Refutation of Two Objections on Sound and Celestial Sounds
Refutes two objections on sound propagation and denies celestial sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections regarding sound propagation and the philosophical refutation of celestial sounds based on the necessity of a material medium.

Core Idea
Sound requires a material medium; celestial spheres are beyond air, so no sound can reach us.

The author addresses two objections about how sound travels through air and then disputes ancient claims of hearing celestial harmonies. This passage clarifies the physical conditions necessary for sound and why celestial sounds are impossible according to philosophical principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author presents two objections and then a refutation of celestial sounds.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the two objections mentioned at the beginning. The first concerns the undulating air losing its shape when entering narrow passages. The second concerns how sound subsists in the air and whether it is heard by one or many.
3
Analyze the refutation of celestial sounds
The author argues that sound and hearing are conditional upon air. Since the celestial spheres are beyond the realm of air, they cannot produce sound, and even if they could, it could not reach us.
The story of Pythagoras is given as an example of the claim being refuted.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why the author insists on air as a medium. This connects to the broader philosophical understanding of sound as a phenomenon requiring a material carrier.
This principle is used to dismiss the possibility of celestial sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى دفع إشكالين : أحدهما أن الهواء المتموج يمتنع أن يبقى على هيئته، من تقصيعات الحروف وتشكيلاتها، عند دخوله في المنافذ الضيقة، ومصادماته للجدران الصلبة . وثانيهما: أن الهواء الحامل للصوت، إن قام الصوت بمجموعه ، لزم أن لا يسمعه إلا واحد من الحاضرين، لأنه بمجموعه لا يصل إلا إلى صماخ واحد، وإن قام بكل جزء منه لزم أن يسمعه كل سامع مراراً بعدد ما يتأدى اليه من أجزاء الهواء المتموج . [قال (فما يحكى) من سماع الأصوات الفلكية لا يستقيم على الأصول الفلسفية]. يعني إن كان حدوث الصوت وسماعه مشروطين بالهواء، لم يكن للفلك صوت، ولو فرض لم يمكن وصوله إلينا لامتناع النفوذ في جرم الفلك، لكن نسب إلى القدماء من الأساطين، أنهم يثبتون للفلكيات، أصوات عجيبة، ونغمات غريبة ، يتحير من سماعها العقل، وتتعجب منها النفس، وحكى عن فيثاغورس، أنه عرج بنفسه إلى العالم العلوي، فسمع بصفاء جوهر نفسه، وذكاء قلبه ، ونغمات الأفلاك، وأصوات حركات الكواكب، ثم رجع إلى استعمال القوى البدنية، ورتب عليها الألحان والنغمات وكمل علم الموسيقى.
Indication of refuting two problems: The first is that the undulating air cannot remain in its state, from the shaping of letters and their formations, when entering narrow passages and colliding with solid walls. And the second: that the air carrying the sound, if the sound subsists in its entirety, then necessarily only one of those present would hear it, because in its entirety it only reaches one ear, and if it subsists in each part of it, then necessarily every hearer would hear it multiple times according to what reaches him from the parts of the undulating air. He said: 'What is narrated about hearing celestial sounds is not consistent with philosophical principles.' Meaning, if the occurrence of sound and its hearing are conditional upon air, then the celestial spheres have no sound, and even if it were assumed, its reaching us would not be possible due to the impossibility of penetration into the celestial body. But it is attributed to the ancient masters that they affirm for celestial beings wondrous sounds and strange melodies, at hearing which the intellect is bewildered and the soul marvels. And it is narrated about Pythagoras that he ascended with his soul to the higher world and heard, by the purity of his essence and the acuteness of his heart, the melodies of the spheres and the sounds of the movements of the planets, then he returned to the use of bodily powers and arranged upon them the melodies and tunes and completed the science of music.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage begins by indicating a refutation of two objections regarding the propagation of sound. The first objection states that undulating air cannot retain its specific formations (such as the shapes of letters) when entering narrow passages and colliding with solid walls. The second objection concerns the carrier of sound: if sound subsists in the entire mass of air, then only one listener would hear it, because the whole mass can only reach one ear; but if sound subsists in every part of the air, then each listener would hear it multiple times, once from each part reaching him. The author then addresses a claim attributed to ancient philosophers about hearing the sounds of celestial spheres. He states that this claim is inconsistent with philosophical principles, because sound and its hearing are conditional upon air. Since the celestial spheres are beyond the realm of air, they cannot produce sound, and even if they could, it could not reach us due to the impossibility of penetration into the celestial body. The author then narrates the story of Pythagoras, who allegedly ascended spiritually to the celestial world and heard the harmonies of the spheres, then returned to the physical world and used that experience to complete the science of music. This story serves as an example of the claim that the author refutes.

هواء
تموج
إشكال
فيثاغورس
أصوات فلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial spheres produce sounds that can be heard by humans.
Correction: Sound requires a material medium like air; the celestial realm is devoid of air, so no sound can occur or propagate to us.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason celestial sounds cannot be heard by humans?
A) The celestial spheres are too far away.
B) Sound and hearing are conditional upon air, and the celestial spheres are beyond air.
C) The human ear is not capable of perceiving such sounds.
D) The celestial spheres do not produce any sound at all.

Key Takeaway
Sound requires a material medium like air; thus celestial sounds, if they exist, cannot reach us, contrary to ancient philosophical claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a wondrous claim about spiritual experiences without first considering whether it aligns with the physical realities that Allah has established? How will you change your approach to evaluating such claims after this lesson?
?
The two objections in this passage show the importance of rigorous analysis: breaking down assumptions and following consequences. In your own intellectual life, are you satisfied with surface-level understanding, or do you strive to dissect arguments as the author does here? What concrete step will you take this week to think more critically about a belief you hold?
?
The story of Pythagoras suggests that even a great philosopher can be mistaken when he ignores the conditions Allah placed on creation. Do you trust your own spiritual experiences more than the clear principles of the created world? How might this lesson guard you against self-deception?

Definition and Theories of Vision
Definition of sight and enumeration of theories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of vision, the various classical theories of how sight occurs, and the author's conclusion that the true nature of vision is solely by God's creation.

Core Idea
Vision is a power at the optic junction; its mechanism is debated, but the truth is divine creation.

This passage introduces the sense of sight and presents multiple competing theories from classical optics and philosophy. The author lists them before affirming the Ash'ari position that vision is ultimately a direct act of Allah. Understanding these theories helps clarify the theological stance on causality and perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of sight
Read the opening sentence that defines visual power (basar) as a faculty at the confluence of two nerves. Notice its function: perceiving colors, lights, and other visible things.
The definition is concise and assumes basic knowledge of anatomy.
2
Identify the five theories
The author lists five theories of vision: impression (insibā'), ray emission (shu'ā'), uniform ray with fluctuation, mediated air, and mere confrontation. Find each one in the text and note their key features.
These theories represent different philosophical schools: some are materialist, others more abstract.
3
Notice the author's truth statement
After presenting the theories, the author says 'the truth is that it is by the sheer creation of God.' This is a theological assertion that transcends physical explanations.
This reflects the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism: all events are directly caused by Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها البصر) وهي قوة في ملتقى العصبتين المفترقتين إلى العينين، يرى بها الألوان والأضواء وغيرهما، بانطباع شبح المرئي في جزء من الرطوبة الجليدية، فيكون المرئي هو الشيء المنطبع شبحه ولا يمتنع اختلافهما في المقدار، أو بخروج الشعاع على هيئة مخروط مصمت، أو مؤتلف من خطوط مجتمعة ، فيما يلي الرأس متفرقة في ما يلي القاعدة، وقيل على استواء مع اضطراب طرفه على المرئي، وقيل بتوسط الهواء المتكيف بشعاع البصر، وقيل بمجرد المقابلة على شرائطها من غير انطباع ولا شعاع والحق أنه بمحض خلق الله تعالى . وقد تقرر في علم التشريح، أنه ينبت من الدماغ أزواج سبعة من العصب. فالزوج الأول، مبدؤه من غور البطنين المقدمين من الدماغ، عند جوار الزائدتين الشبيهتين بحلمتي الثدي وهو صغير مجوف يتيامن النابت منهما يساراً، ويتياسر النابت منهما يميناً، ثم يلتقيان على تقاطع صليبين، ثم ينفذ النابت يميناً إلى الحدقة اليمنى، والنابت يساراً إلى الحدقة اليسرى. والدليل على كون القوى المدركة في المحال المذكورة، هو توجب الافة في تلك القوى . واختلفوا في كيفية الإبصار. فقيل : إنه بانطباع شبح المرئي في جزء من الرطوبة الجليدية التي تشبه البرد والجمد، فإنها مثل مرآة، فإذا قابلها متلون مضيء، انطبع مثل صورته فيها، كما ينطبع صورة الإنسان في المرآة. لا بأن ينفصل من المتلون شيء، ويمتد إلى العين. بل بأن يحدث مثل
He said: 'And among them is sight.' It is a power at the confluence of the two nerves diverging to the eyes, by which one sees colors, lights, and other things, by the impression of the image of the seen object in a part of the vitreous humor. So the seen object is the thing whose image is impressed, and it is not impossible for them to differ in magnitude. Or by the emission of a ray in the form of a solid cone, or composed of converging lines near the head and diverging at the base. And it is said to be on a uniform [ray] with its end fluctuating over the seen object. And it is said by the mediation of air conditioned by the visual ray. And it is said by mere confrontation under its conditions, without impression or ray. And the truth is that it is by the sheer creation of God, Most High. And it has been established in the science of anatomy that seven pairs of nerves grow from the brain. The first pair has its origin from the depth of the two anterior ventricles of the brain, near the two appendages resembling nipples; it is small and hollow; the one growing from them inclines to the right leftwards, and the one growing from them inclines to the left rightwards. Then they meet in a cross intersection, then the growth proceeds on the right to the right pupil, and the growth on the left to the left pupil. And the evidence that the perceiving powers are in the mentioned places is that damage necessitates [impairment] in those powers. And they differed concerning the modality of sight. It was said: it is by the impression of the image of the seen object in a part of the vitreous humor, which resembles cold and ice; it is like a mirror. When a colored luminous object faces it, the like of its image is impressed in it, just as the image of a person is impressed in a mirror. Not by something separating from the colored object and extending to the eye. Rather, by the occurrence of the like.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines vision and discusses its various theories and underlying anatomy. It begins with a definition: vision is a power located at the confluence of the two optic nerves that diverge to the eyes. Through this power, colors, lights, and other visible things are perceived. The author then lists several theories of how vision occurs: (1) by the impression (insibā') of the image of the seen object in a part of the vitreous humor (the crystalline lens); (2) by the emission of a ray (shu'ā') in the form of a solid cone, or composed of lines that converge near the head and diverge at the base; (3) by a uniform ray with a fluctuating end that scans the object; (4) by the mediation of air conditioned by the visual ray; (5) by mere confrontation (muqābala) under certain conditions, without impression or ray. The author then states that the truth is that vision occurs purely by the creation of God (bi-mahḍ khalq Allāh). Next, anatomical evidence is presented: from the brain emerge seven pairs of nerves. The first pair originates deep within the two anterior ventricles, near two appendages resembling nipples; these nerves are small and hollow, and they cross each other before reaching the eyes. The evidence that the perceiving powers are located in these places is that damage to them impairs those powers. Finally, the author returns to the theories of vision, elaborating on the impression theory: the vitreous humor acts like a mirror, and when a colored luminous object faces it, its image is impressed in it, without any physical separation from the object. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence, likely continued on the next page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision requires a physical ray leaving the eye to strike the object.
Correction: The author lists ray emission as one theory, but he affirms that the true nature of vision is by divine creation, not by any physical mechanism.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's final position on the true nature of vision?
A) Vision occurs by the impression of the image in the vitreous humor.
B) Vision occurs by the emission of a ray from the eye.
C) Vision occurs by mere confrontation without impression or ray.
D) Vision occurs purely by the creation of God.

Key Takeaway
The author presents multiple theories of vision but settles on the Ash'ari view that sight occurs by direct divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you open your eyes and see the world, do your thoughts immediately go to light, optics, and the eye's physiology, or do you remember that Allah is directly creating this sight for you in that very moment? How can you constantly remind yourself of His immediate action in your perception?
?
In your daily life, when you see something that delights or shocks you, do you ever pause to thank the Creator for that specific act of seeing? If not, what practical step can you take today to shift your awareness from the means to the Maker?
?
The passage lists five different theories of vision, but the truth is that it is purely Allah's creation. Are you tempted to rely on scientific explanations as if they are the ultimate cause? How does this affect your trust in Allah's absolute control over every detail of your existence?

Anatomical Evidence and the Impression Theory
Anatomical description of optic nerves and elaboration of the impression theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the anatomical basis of vision as described by classical scholars and the theory of impression of the image in the vitreous humor.

Core Idea
The optic nerves cross at the brain; vision occurs by impression of the object's image in the eye's vitreous humor, like a mirror.

After stating that vision is by God's creation, the author provides anatomical evidence from the brain's structure. He then elaborates on the impression theory, comparing the vitreous humor to a mirror. This lesson connects physiology with philosophical theories of perception.

Guided Path
1
Study the anatomical description
Read the description of the first pair of optic nerves: their origin deep in the brain, their crossing, and their path to the pupils. Note the details about the two appendages.
This anatomy is based on classical dissection, but the essential idea of nerve crossing is correct.
2
Understand the evidence from damage
The author argues that damage to these nerves impairs vision, proving that the power of sight resides in those places.
This is an early form of localization of brain function.
3
Analyze the impression theory
The author explains the impression theory in detail: the vitreous humor acts as a mirror, and an image is imprinted without any physical transfer of matter from the object.
This theory avoids the problem of material transfer while explaining how we see.

Misconception: The impression theory requires that something physically separates from the object and travels to the eye.
Correction: The author explicitly denies this: 'Not by something separating from the colored object and extending to the eye.' The image is generated by a direct impression.

C) They fuse into a single nerve before reaching the eyes.
D) They branch into multiple fibers that spread throughout the eye.

Key Takeaway
The anatomical description supports the impression theory, where the image is imprinted like a mirror, without material transfer.

?
The ancient scholars dissected bodies to uncover this intricate nerve crossing. What have you personally sacrificed to gain deeper knowledge of Allah's creation? ?
If your vision is impaired by damage to these nerves, your perception of reality is altered. How does this fragility of your senses remind you that your knowledge is dependent on Allah's permission? Will you start acknowledging Him every time you see clearly?

Definition of Sight and Theories of Vision
Taftazani defines sight and lists five theories of vision, concluding with divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the power of sight according to Taftazani and the various theories of vision, including the conclusion that the ultimate cause is divine creation.

Core Idea
Vision theories abound, but the truth is that all is by God's direct creation.

Taftazani begins by defining the faculty of sight and then surveys the main physical theories proposed by scholars. He presents each theory concisely, from imprint of the phantasm to emission of rays, and finally asserts that the true mechanism is simply by God's creation. This passage demonstrates how Islamic theologians approach physical phenomena: they acknowledge natural descriptions but anchor certainty in divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of sight
Read the Arabic passage once. Focus on the opening definition: sight is a power at the meeting point of two nerves, by which one sees colors, lights, etc.
Note the term 'رطوبة جليدية' (ice-like moisture) which is key in the first theory.
2
Identify each theory of vision
The passage lists five theories: imprint of phantasm, emission of a ray cone, agitation at the edge, mediation of conditioned air, and mere opposition. Find each one in the text.
The theories are introduced by 'أو' (or) and 'وقيل' (it was said).
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends with 'والحق أنه بمحض خلق الله تعالى' meaning the truth is by God's pure creation. Reflect on how this conclusion ties the physical theories to divine causality.
This is a key principle in Ash'ari theology: natural causes are only customary, not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها البصر) وهي قوة في ملتقى العصبتين المفترقتين إلى العينين، يرى بها الألوان والأضواء وغيرهما، بانطباع شبح المرئي في جزء من الرطوبة الجليدية، فيكون المرئي هو الشيء المنطبع شبحه ولا يمتنع اختلافهما في المقدار، أو بخروج الشعاع على هيئة مخروط مصمت، أو مؤتلف من خطوط مجتمعة، فيما يلي الرأس متفرقة في ما يلي القاعدة، وقيل على استواء مع اضطراب طرفه على المرئي، وقيل بتوسط الهواء المتكيف بشعاع البصر، وقيل بمجرد المقابلة على شرائطها من غير انطباع ولا شعاع والحق أنه بمحض خلق الله تعالى .
He said (and among them is sight). It is a power at the meeting point of the two nerves branching to the eyes, by which one sees colors, lights, and others, through the imprint of the phantasm of the visible object in a part of the ice-like moisture, so the visible object is the thing whose phantasm is imprinted, and it is not impossible for the two to differ in magnitude, or by the emission of a ray in the form of a solid cone, or composed of lines converging at the side near the head and diverging at the side near the base, and it was said [it is] on a level with agitation of its edge upon the visible object, and it was said by the mediation of the air conditioned by the ray of sight, and it was said by mere opposition with its conditions without imprint or ray, and the truth is that it is by sheer creation of God, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines the power of sight (basar) and surveys the main theories of vision discussed by scholars. The author begins by giving the definition: sight is a power located at the meeting point of the two optic nerves, through which colors, lights, and other visible things are perceived. He then briefly describes the mechanism according to each theory: (1) imprint of the phantasm (shabah) of the visible object in the ice-like moisture of the eye; (2) emission of a ray (shuā') from the eye in the form of a solid cone or a bundle of lines; (3) a theory of agitation upon the object; (4) mediation of the air conditioned by the ray; (5) mere opposition with conditions. The author concludes by stating that the truth (al-ḥaqq) is that vision occurs purely by God's direct creation, implying that the physical accounts are merely descriptions of the usual divine practice.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Taftazani endorses a specific physical theory of vision.
Correction: He lists them only as reported opinions, then clarifies that the true cause is God's direct creation, not any physical mechanism.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, what is the ultimate truth about the mechanism of vision?
A) It is by imprint of the phantasm.
B) It is by emission of a ray.
C) It is by sheer creation of God.
D) It is by mere opposition with conditions.

Key Takeaway
The true cause of vision is God's direct creation, not any physical process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something beautiful, do you ever pause to acknowledge that the very ability to see is a direct creation of God at that moment, or do you take it for granted as a mere biological process? What would change if you consciously remembered this every time you opened your eyes?
?
The author lists five physical theories and then dismisses them all as the ultimate cause. In your own life, do you ever attribute outcomes to secondary causes (effort, skill, chance) and forget that the true cause is God's direct act? How can you shift your mindset to see God's creation in every moment?
?
If vision is purely by God's creation, what does that imply about your responsibility to use your sight in obedience to Him? How does this lesson challenge you to guard your gaze and use your eyes for what pleases God?

The Imprint Theory of Vision: Objections and Answers
Taftazani expounds the imprint theory of vision and refutes two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the imprint theory of vision as advocated by Taftazani, including the two objections raised against it and their refutations.

Core Idea
Vision occurs by imprint of the object's phantasm in the eye, like a mirror.

Taftazani delves into the mechanics of vision, presenting a detailed theory that explains how we perceive external objects. He then engages with potential critiques, defending the theory by clarifying what is truly seen and addressing concerns about size. This passage is a model of how kalam arguments are constructed: theory, objection, rejoinder.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the imprint theory
Find the description of vision as imprint of the phantasm in the ice-like moisture. Note the mirror analogy.
3
Note the first objection
The objection claims that if we see an imprint, we see only an image, not the object itself.
4
Examine the first answer
The answer distinguishes between the imprinted phantasm and the object that causes it. The visible object is the source.
5
Examine the second objection and answer
The second objection concerns size; the answer points to the example of a small mirror reflecting a large face, showing shape and color need not match size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في كيفية الإبصار. فقيل : إنه بانطباع شبح المرئي في جزء من الرطوبة الجليدية التي تشبه البرد والجمد، فإنها مثل مرآة، فإذا قابلها متلون مضيء، انطبع مثل صورته فيها، كما ينطبع صورة الإنسان في المرآة. لا بأن ينفصل من المتلون شيء، ويمتد إلى العين. بل بأن يحدث مثل صورته في المرآة، وفي عين الناظر، ويكون استعداد حصوله بالمقابلة المخصوصة، مع توسط الهواء المشف . ولما اعترض على هذا بوجهين أحدهما أن المرئي حينئذ يكون صورة الشيء وشبحه لا نفسه، ونحن قاطعون بأنا نرى نفس هذا الملون . وثانيهما : أن شبح الشيء مساوله في المقدار، وإلا لم يكن صورة له ومثالاً . وحينئذ يلزم أن لا نرى ما هو أعظم من الجليدية، لأن امتناع انطباع العظيم في الصغير معلوم بالضرورة. أشار إلى الجواب بأنه إذا كان رؤية الشيء بانطباع شبحه، كان المرئي هو الذي انطبع شبحه لا نفس الشبح، كما مر في العلم ، وبأن شبح الشيء ، لا يلزم أن يساويه في المقدار كما يشاهد من صورة الوجه في المرآة الصغيرة. إذ المراد به ما يناسب الشيء في الشكل واللون دون المقدار، غاية الأمر أنا لا نعرف [؟لمبة] إبصار الشيء العظيم، وإدراك البعد بينه وبين المرئي، بمجرد انطباع صورة صغيرة منه في الجليدية وتأديها بواسطة الزوح المصبوب في العصبتين إلى الباصرة.
And they differed concerning the modality of vision. It was said: it is by the imprint of the phantasm of the visible object in a part of the ice-like moisture that resembles cold and ice, for it is like a mirror, so when a colored luminous object faces it, the like of its form is imprinted in it, just as the form of a person is imprinted in a mirror. Not by something separating from the colored object and extending to the eye. Rather, by the occurrence of the like of its form in the mirror and in the eye of the beholder, and the readiness for its occurrence is through the specific opposition, with the mediation of transparent air. And when this was objected to on two grounds: one, that then the visible object is the form and phantasm of the thing, not itself, while we are certain that we see this colored thing itself. And second: that the phantasm of a thing equals it in magnitude, otherwise it would not be a form and likeness of it. Then it would follow that we do not see what is larger than the ice-like moisture, because the impossibility of imprinting the large in the small is known necessarily. He indicated the answer: that if vision of a thing is by imprint of its phantasm, then the visible object is that whose phantasm is imprinted, not the phantasm itself, as has passed in knowledge; and that the phantasm of a thing does not necessarily equal it in magnitude, as is observed from the image of the face in a small mirror. For what is meant is that which corresponds to the thing in shape and color, not in magnitude. The utmost is that we do not know the [؟] of seeing the large thing, and perceiving the distance between it and the visible object, merely through the imprint of a small image of it in the ice-like moisture and its conveyance via the spirit poured into the two nerves to the visual faculty.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the imprint theory (inbisāṭ al-shabah) in detail, followed by two objections and their answers. The theory holds that vision occurs when the phantasm (shabah) of an external object is imprinted in the ice-like moisture (al-ruṭūba al-jālīdiyya) of the eye, much like an image forms in a mirror. The first objection argues that if we see only the imprinted phantasm, then we are seeing a mere representation, not the object itself, contrary to our certainty that we see the actual object. The second objection contends that the phantasm must be equal in size to the object to be a true likeness, but then a large object could not be imprinted in the small eye. The answer to the first objection clarifies that the visible object is the thing whose phantasm is imprinted—not the phantasm itself—so we directly perceive the object through its imprint. To the second, the answer is that the phantasm need not match the object in magnitude, only in shape and color, as seen when a small mirror reflects a large face. The author notes that we may not fully understand how a small image enables perception of size and distance, but the theory accounts for it via the conveyed impression to the visual center.

Key Terms
انطباع
شبح
رطوبة جليدية
مرآة
مقابلة
هواء مشف
باصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imprint theory reduces vision to seeing an image, so we never see the actual object.
Correction: The author clarifies that the visible object is that whose phantasm is imprinted, not the phantasm itself, so we directly perceive the object via its imprint.

Checkpoint
How does the author's answer respond to the objection that we would see only the image, not the object itself?
A) It concedes that we see only the image.
B) It says the visible is the imprinted phantasm's source, not the phantasm.
C) It denies that any imprint occurs.
D) It says the object is recreated in the eye.

Key Takeaway
The imprint theory explains vision as the imprint of the object's phantasm in the eye, and the objections concerning seeing only an image and size mismatch are resolved by clarifying that the visible object is the source of the imprint and that the phantasm need not match size.

The Emission Theory of Vision and Its Variants
Taftazani presents the emission theory of vision and its different formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four variants of the emission theory of vision as reported by Taftazani.

Core Idea
The emission theory of vision had many forms, from solid cones to conditioned air.

This passage presents a classic natural-philosophy debate about how vision works. Taftazani reports four different versions of the emission theory, each with a different description of the ray emitted from the eye.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the general claim, then four variants introduced by 'then they differed' and 'and it was said'.
Pay attention to the key terms: شعاع, مخروط, etc.
2
Identify the four variants
List each variant: solid cone, composed lines, level ray with oscillation, and air conditioning. Note how each differs from the basic cone model.
The first variant is the solid cone; second is composed lines; third is level and oscillating; fourth conditions the air.
3
Compare the variants
Consider what each variant implies about the nature of the ray. Which is more physical? Which introduces a role for the medium?
These differences reflect deeper debates in natural philosophy.
4
Reflect on context
Think about why Taftazani reports these views without endorsing any. This is typical of his approach in the Maqasid: presenting views before critiquing them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إن الإبصار بخروج شعاع من العين على هيئة مخروط رأسه عند العين، وقاعدته عند المرئي. ثم اختلفوا في أن ذلك المخروط مصمت، أو مؤتلف من خطوط مجتمعة في الجانب الذي يلي الرأس متفرقة في الجانب الذي يلي القاعدة، وقيل : لا على هيئة المخروط بل على استواء، لكن يثبت طرفه الذي على العين، ويضطرب طرفه الاخر على المرئي . وقيل : الشعاع الذي في العين يكيف الهواء بكيفيته، ويصير الكل آلة في الإبصار.
And it was said: sight is by the emission of a ray from the eye in the form of a cone, its apex at the eye and its base at the visible object. Then they differed concerning whether that cone is solid, or composed of lines converging on the side near the head and diverging on the side near the base, and it was said: not in the form of a cone but on a level, but its end at the eye is fixed and its other end oscillates upon the visible object. And it was said: the ray that is in the eye conditions the air with its quality, and the whole becomes an instrument in vision.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage outlines the emission theory (khurūj shu‘ā‘) and its variations. According to this theory, vision occurs by a ray that emerges from the eye in the shape of a cone (makhrūṭ) whose apex is at the eye and base at the visible object. Scholars differed on the nature of this cone: some said it is solid (muṣmat), others that it is composed of lines that converge near the head and diverge toward the base. A third opinion rejected the cone shape entirely, claiming the ray extends on a level plane with the end at the eye fixed and the other end oscillating over the object. A fourth variant held that the ray conditions the surrounding air with its quality, so that the entire medium becomes a visual instrument. These details reflect the natural philosophy debates of the time, which the author reports without endorsing.

Key Terms
شعاع
مخروط
مصمت
استواء
اضطراب
هواء متكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Emission theory must involve a solid physical ray leaving the eye.
Correction: Some versions propose the ray conditions the air rather than being a solid emission.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a description of the ray in the emission theory as mentioned?
A) A solid cone.
B) A cone composed of converging and diverging lines.
C) A straight line from eye to object.
D) A level ray with one end fixed and the other oscillating.

Key Takeaway
The emission theory of vision was not monolithic; it had multiple formulations debated by philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your eyes every moment, yet the mechanism of vision was debated by scholars. How does knowing that even the physical act of seeing has been subject to multiple interpretations affect your confidence in what you perceive? Are there areas of your life where you assume your perception is infallible? Will you commit to questioning your assumptions about what you 'clearly see' this week?
?
The theories here treat the medium between eye and object as active—either shaped by rays or conditioned by them. Do you consider the 'medium' of your own understanding—your biases, presuppositions, and prior knowledge—as active in shaping what you think you know? Take a moment to identify one assumption you hold about a belief that might be shaping your interpretation of evidence, and resolve to examine its source.

First Argument: The Polished Luminous Eye
Al-Taftazani presents the first argument for impression theory and notes its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first and chief argument for the impression theory of vision—that the eye is a polished luminous body—and why Al-Taftazani argues that it only proves imprinting, not vision itself.

Core Idea
Imprinting an image is not the same as seeing.

Al-Taftazani begins his refutation of the impression theory by addressing its strongest argument. The impressionists claim that the eye, being polished and luminous, receives images like a mirror. They support this with empirical observations about light in the eye. But the refutation is precise: even if an image is imprinted, that does not explain the act of seeing. Let's examine this carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and English text to understand the full flow of the argument.
2
Identify the major and minor premises
The argument has two premises. The major is that every polished luminous body imprints images. The minor is that the eye is such a body.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
Four pieces of empirical evidence are given for the minor premise. List them and consider their validity.
The evidence includes light from rubbing eyes, the back of a black cat, etc.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Taftazani concedes that imprinting occurs but argues that this does not prove that vision is the same as imprinting.
5
Reflect on the logical distinction
Why is the distinction between image formation and the act of seeing important for the overall debate?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك الأولون بوجوه :
أحدها وهو العمدة، أن العين جسم صقيل نوراني، وكل جسم كذلك، إذا قابله كثيف ملون انطبع فيه شبحه كالمرآة. أما الكبرى فظاهرة، وأما الصغرى فلما يشاهد من النور في الظلمة، إذا حك المنتبه من النوم عينه، وكذا عند إمرار اليد على ظهر الهرة السوداء، ولأن الإنسان إذا نظر نحو أنفه قد يرى عليه دائرة من الضياء، وإذا انتبه من النوم، قد يبصر ما قرب منه زماناً، ثم يفقده، وذلك لامتلاء العين من النور في ذلك الوقت، وإذا غمض إحدى العينين، يتسع ثقبة العين الأخرى، وما ذاك إلا لأن جوهراً نورانياً يملأه، ولأنه لولا انصباب الأرواح النورانية من الدماغ إلى العين لما جعلت ثقبتا الإبصار مجوفتين، وهذا بعد تمامه، إنما يفيد انطباع الشبح لا كون الإبصار به.
وثانيها : أن سائر الحواس إنما تدرك بأن يأتي صورة المحسوس إليها، لا بأن يخرج منها شيء إلى المحسوس، فكذا الإبصار. ورد بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع .
وثالثها: أن من نظر إلى الشمس طويلاً، ثم أعرض عنها تبقى صورتها في عينه زماناً، وردّ بأن الصورة في خياله، لا عينه، كما إذا أغمض العين.
ورابعها أن الشيء بعينه إذا قرب من الرائي يرى أكبر مما إذا بعد عنه، وما ذاك إلا لأن الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء المشف رأسه متصل بالحدقة وقاعدته سطح المرئي، حتى أنه وتر لزاوية المخروط، ومعلوم أن وتر الزاوية كلما قرب من الزاوية، كان الساق أصغر، والزاوية أعظم - وكلما بعد فبالعكس، والشبح الذي في الزاوية الكبرى، أعظم من الذي في الصغرى، وهذا إنما يستقيم إذا جعلنا موضع الإبصار هو الزاوية على ما هو رأي الانطباع، لا القاعدة على ما هو رأي خروج الشعاع فإنها لا تتفاوت . ورد بأن لا نسلم أنه لا سبب سوى ذلك. كيف وأصحاب الشعاع أيضاً يثبتون سببه، على أن استلزام عظم زاوية الرؤية عظم المرئي وصغرها صغره محل نظر.
وإلى ما ذكرنا من وجوه الرد أشار بقوله وهو ضعيف .
The first group adhered to [the impression theory] by [presenting] aspects:
One of them—and it is the chief one—is that the eye is a polished, luminous body, and every such body, when it faces an opaque colored object, its image is imprinted in it like a mirror. As for the major premise, it is obvious; as for the minor premise, [it is supported] by the light seen in darkness when one who awakens from sleep rubs his eye, and likewise when passing the hand over the back of a black cat, and because when a person looks toward his nose he may see a circle of brightness upon it, and when he awakens from sleep he may see what is near him for a time then lose it, and that is because the eye is filled with light at that time, and when he closes one eye, the pupil of the other eye dilates—and that is only because a luminous substance fills it, and because if the luminous spirits did not flow from the brain to the eye, the two openings of sight would not have been made hollow. But after its completion, this only proves the imprinting of the image, not that sight occurs by means of it.
A second [argument]: that the other senses only perceive by the image of the sensible coming to them, not by something exiting from them to the sensible, so likewise [for] sight. It is refuted by [saying] it is an analogy without common ground.
A third [argument]: that whoever looks at the sun for a long time then turns away, its image remains in his eye for a time. It is refuted by [saying] the image is in his imagination, not his eye, as when he closes the eye.
A fourth [argument]: that the very same thing, when near to the observer, is seen larger than when far away—and that is only because the imprint is on a cone of transparent air, its apex connected to the pupil and its base to the surface of the visible object, such that it is the chord of the angle of the cone, and it is known that the chord of the angle, the closer to the angle, the smaller the leg and the larger the angle—and the farther, the opposite, and the image at the larger angle is larger than that at the smaller. This only holds if we make the locus of sight the angle, as is the opinion of the impression [theory], not the base, as is the opinion of the emission of the ray, for it does not vary. It is refuted by [saying] we do not concede that there is no cause other than that; how [can that be] when the proponents of the ray also establish its cause, and indeed the implication that the largeness of the visual angle entails the largeness of the visible [object] and its smallness entails its smallness is a matter of scrutiny.
And to what we have mentioned of the refutations, he alluded by his saying: 'and it is weak.'
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes four arguments advanced by proponents of the impression theory (al-intiba') of vision. The author begins by stating that the first group (the impressionists) adhere to their theory by presenting several 'aspects' (wujuh), i.e., arguments.

**First argument (the chief one):** The eye is a polished, luminous body. Whenever such a body faces an opaque colored object, the object's image is imprinted in it, like a mirror. The major premise (every polished luminous body does this) is obvious. The minor premise (the eye is such a body) is supported by empirical observations: light seen when rubbing the eyes after sleep, a circle of brightness seen when looking toward the nose, dilation of the pupil, and the flow of luminous spirits from brain to eye. However, the author notes after summarizing this argument that it only proves the imprinting of an image, not that sight occurs by means of that imprint.

**Second argument:** By analogy with the other senses, which perceive by receiving the image of the sensible, sight must also work by reception. The refutation: this is an analogy (tamthil) without a common ground (jami'), meaning the analogy is invalid.

**Third argument:** Afterimages: when looking at the sun and turning away, the image remains in the eye. The refutation: the image is in the imagination (khayal), not the eye, as when one closes the eye and still sees an afterimage.

**Fourth argument:** The same object appears larger when near and smaller when far, explained by the geometry of a cone: the image is imprinted on a cone of transparent air, and the visual angle determines size. This only works if the locus of sight is the apex (as impression theory holds), not if it is the base (as in ray theory). The refutation: there may be other causes, and the claimed necessity that angle size determines apparent size is open to scrutiny.

The author concludes by saying that he has alluded to the refutations and that the impression theory is weak (da'if).

Key Terms
الانطباع (al-intiba')
الجسم الصقيل (al-jism al-saqil)
شبح (shabah)
الإبصار (al-ibsar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impression theory is refuted by denying that the eye is polished or luminous.
Correction: Al-Taftazani does not deny the evidence; he accepts that imprinting occurs but argues that this does not prove that vision is the same as imprinting.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the first argument for the impression theory is deemed insufficient?
A) The eye is not actually polished.
B) It only proves image imprinting, not that vision occurs by that imprint.
C) The analogy with the mirror is flawed.
D) The evidence from rubbing the eyes is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for impression theory establishes image imprinting but not vision, thus the theory remains unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that Al-Taftazani accepted the physical evidence but rejected the conclusion that it proves vision itself. Think of something in your life where you accepted a conclusion because it seemed obvious, but later realized the evidence didn't actually support it. What was that situation, and how does this lesson challenge you to examine your own reasoning more carefully?
?
The passage distinguishes between the physical imprint of an image and the act of seeing. In your own daily experience, do you tend to treat your senses as passive receivers of reality or as active interpreters? How might this distinction affect the way you approach doubts about what you perceive?
?
Consider a belief you hold strongly—about faith, knowledge, or yourself—that you think is proven by certain evidence. Imagine showing that evidence to a critical thinker like Al-Taftazani. Would they agree that the evidence proves what you think? If not, what would need to be added? This lesson calls you to examine the gap between evidence and conclusion in your own convictions. What will you do with that call?

Second Argument: Analogy with Other Senses
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the analogical argument for impression theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the impression theory based on analogy with other senses and why Al-Taftazani rejects it as a invalid analogy.

Core Idea
Analogy without a shared essential property proves nothing.

The second argument for impression theory relies on an analogy with the other senses. Just as hearing and smell receive impressions, so must sight. Al-Taftazani's refutation is brief but decisive: the analogy lacks a shared essential property (jami'), making it invalid in rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the short Arabic and English passage to understand the analogical reasoning and the response.
2
Identify the analogy
The argument draws an analogy between sight and the other senses. Identify what is being compared.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Taftazani rejects the analogy by saying it lacks a 'jami'' (common ground). Why is that fatal?
In kalam, analogies require a shared underlying cause to be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك الأولون بوجوه :
أحدها وهو العمدة، أن العين جسم صقيل نوراني، وكل جسم كذلك، إذا قابله كثيف ملون انطبع فيه شبحه كالمرآة. أما الكبرى فظاهرة، وأما الصغرى فلما يشاهد من النور في الظلمة، إذا حك المنتبه من النوم عينه، وكذا عند إمرار اليد على ظهر الهرة السوداء، ولأن الإنسان إذا نظر نحو أنفه قد يرى عليه دائرة من الضياء، وإذا انتبه من النوم، قد يبصر ما قرب منه زماناً، ثم يفقده، وذلك لامتلاء العين من النور في ذلك الوقت، وإذا غمض إحدى العينين، يتسع ثقبة العين الأخرى، وما ذاك إلا لأن جوهراً نورانياً يملأه، ولأنه لولا انصباب الأرواح النورانية من الدماغ إلى العين لما جعلت ثقبتا الإبصار مجوفتين، وهذا بعد تمامه، إنما يفيد انطباع الشبح لا كون الإبصار به.
وثانيها : أن سائر الحواس إنما تدرك بأن يأتي صورة المحسوس إليها، لا بأن يخرج منها شيء إلى المحسوس، فكذا الإبصار. ورد بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع .
وثالثها: أن من نظر إلى الشمس طويلاً، ثم أعرض عنها تبقى صورتها في عينه زماناً، وردّ بأن الصورة في خياله، لا عينه، كما إذا أغمض العين.
ورابعها أن الشيء بعينه إذا قرب من الرائي يرى أكبر مما إذا بعد عنه، وما ذاك إلا لأن الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء المشف رأسه متصل بالحدقة وقاعدته سطح المرئي، حتى أنه وتر لزاوية المخروط، ومعلوم أن وتر الزاوية كلما قرب من الزاوية، كان الساق أصغر، والزاوية أعظم - وكلما بعد فبالعكس، والشبح الذي في الزاوية الكبرى، أعظم من الذي في الصغرى، وهذا إنما يستقيم إذا جعلنا موضع الإبصار هو الزاوية على ما هو رأي الانطباع، لا القاعدة على ما هو رأي خروج الشعاع فإنها لا تتفاوت . ورد بأن لا نسلم أنه لا سبب سوى ذلك. كيف وأصحاب الشعاع أيضاً يثبتون سببه، على أن استلزام عظم زاوية الرؤية عظم المرئي وصغرها صغره محل نظر.
وإلى ما ذكرنا من وجوه الرد أشار بقوله وهو ضعيف .
The first group adhered to [the impression theory] by [presenting] aspects:
One of them—and it is the chief one—is that the eye is a polished, luminous body, and every such body, when it faces an opaque colored object, its image is imprinted in it like a mirror. As for the major premise, it is obvious; as for the minor premise, [it is supported] by the light seen in darkness when one who awakens from sleep rubs his eye, and likewise when passing the hand over the back of a black cat, and because when a person looks toward his nose he may see a circle of brightness upon it, and when he awakens from sleep he may see what is near him for a time then lose it, and that is because the eye is filled with light at that time, and when he closes one eye, the pupil of the other eye dilates—and that is only because a luminous substance fills it, and because if the luminous spirits did not flow from the brain to the eye, the two openings of sight would not have been made hollow. But after its completion, this only proves the imprinting of the image, not that sight occurs by means of it.
A second [argument]: that the other senses only perceive by the image of the sensible coming to them, not by something exiting from them to the sensible, so likewise [for] sight. It is refuted by [saying] it is an analogy without common ground.
A third [argument]: that whoever looks at the sun for a long time then turns away, its image remains in his eye for a time. It is refuted by [saying] the image is in his imagination, not his eye, as when he closes the eye.
A fourth [argument]: that the very same thing, when near to the observer, is seen larger than when far away—and that is only because the imprint is on a cone of transparent air, its apex connected to the pupil and its base to the surface of the visible object, such that it is the chord of the angle of the cone, and it is known that the chord of the angle, the closer to the angle, the smaller the leg and the larger the angle—and the farther, the opposite, and the image at the larger angle is larger than that at the smaller. This only holds if we make the locus of sight the angle, as is the opinion of the impression [theory], not the base, as is the opinion of the emission of the ray, for it does not vary. It is refuted by [saying] we do not concede that there is no cause other than that; how [can that be] when the proponents of the ray also establish its cause, and indeed the implication that the largeness of the visual angle entails the largeness of the visible [object] and its smallness entails its smallness is a matter of scrutiny.
And to what we have mentioned of the refutations, he alluded by his saying: 'and it is weak.'

**Second argument:** By analogy with the other senses, which perceive by receiving the image of the sensible, sight must also work by reception. The refutation: this is an analogy (tamthil) without a common ground (jami'), meaning the analogy is invalid.

التمثيل (al-tamthil)
الجامع (al-jami')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogies from other senses are sufficient to prove the impression theory.
Correction: In kalam, analogies require a shared essential property (jami') to be valid; here no such common ground is established.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Taftazani reject the second argument?
A) The other senses actually emit rays.
B) It is an analogy without common ground.
C) The eye is not a sense.
D) The argument is too weak to mention.

Key Takeaway
Analogical arguments for the impression theory fail without a shared essential property between cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that an analogy without common ground is invalid. In your own reasoning about Allah, when have you assumed that something about creation must apply to the Creator simply because it 'seems similar'? What did you base that on, and how will you guard against such analogies now?
?
This passage refutes an argument about how sight works. Reflect: In your daily life, how often do you make judgments about others based on superficial analogies—assuming their intentions are like yours without proof? What would it take to stop and demand a real common ground before concluding?

Third Argument: Afterimages from the Sun
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the afterimage argument for impression theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the impression theory based on afterimages and why Al-Taftazani attributes the persistence to imagination, not the eye.

Core Idea
Afterimages are in the mind, not the eye.

The third argument for impression theory points to afterimages: when you look at the sun and then look away, the image remains. This seems to show that the image is imprinted in the eye. Al-Taftazani's refutation is straightforward: the image is in the imagination, not the eye, as demonstrated by closing the eyes and still seeing the afterimage.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the short passage to understand the claim about afterimages and the response.
2
Understand the phenomenon
The argument uses the common experience of afterimages after viewing a bright light.
3
Analyze the refutation
Al-Taftazani says the image is in the imagination (khayal), not the eye, by analogy with closing the eye and still seeing the image.

?

Fourth Argument: Geometric Change in Size
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the geometric cone argument for impression theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument for the impression theory based on the geometric cone model of vision and why Al-Taftazani questions its necessity and exclusivity.

Core Idea
Geometry alone does not prove impression theory; other explanations exist.

The fourth argument for impression theory relies on a geometric model: the visual cone. When an object is closer, its image covers a larger angle, so the imprint is larger. This seems to support impression theory because the angle's apex is at the eye. Al-Taftazani's refutation is twofold: first, he doubts that only this explanation can account for size changes, and second, he notes that proponents of the ray theory also have an explanation. The connection between angle size and apparent size is not necessarily required.

Guided Path
1
Read the geometric argument
Read the Arabic and English text carefully to understand the cone geometry.
2
Visualize the cone
The cone has its apex at the pupil and its base on the visible object. The chord of the angle determines the image size.
3
Understand the condition
The argument works only if the locus of sight is the angle (as in impression theory), not the base (as in ray theory).
4
Analyze the refutation
Al-Taftazani argues that other causes are possible, and ray theorists also have an explanation, so the argument is not decisive.
He also questions the necessity that angle size determines apparent size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك الأولون بوجوه :
أحدها وهو العمدة، أن العين جسم صقيل نوراني، وكل جسم كذلك، إذا قابله كثيف ملون انطبع فيه شبحه كالمرآة. أما الكبرى فظاهرة، وأما الصغرى فلما يشاهد من النور في الظلمة، إذا حك المنتبه من النوم عينه، وكذا عند إمرار اليد على ظهر الهرة السوداء، ولأن الإنسان إذا نظر نحو أنفه قد يرى عليه دائرة من الضياء، وإذا انتبه من النوم، قد يبصر ما قرب منه زماناً، ثم يفقده، وذلك لامتلاء العين من النور في ذلك الوقت، وإذا غمض إحدى العينين، يتسع ثقبة العين الأخرى، وما ذاك إلا لأن جوهراً نورانياً يملأه، ولأنه لولا انصباب الأرواح النورانية من الدماغ إلى العين لما جعلت ثقبتا الإبصار مجوفتين، وهذا بعد تمامه، إنما يفيد انطباع الشبح لا كون الإبصار به.
وثانيها : أن سائر الحواس إنما تدرك بأن يأتي صورة المحسوس إليها، لا بأن يخرج منها شيء إلى المحسوس، فكذا الإبصار. ورد بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع .
وثالثها: أن من نظر إلى الشمس طويلاً، ثم أعرض عنها تبقى صورتها في عينه زماناً، وردّ بأن الصورة في خياله، لا عينه، كما إذا أغمض العين.
ورابعها أن الشيء بعينه إذا قرب من الرائي يرى أكبر مما إذا بعد عنه، وما ذاك إلا لأن الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء المشف رأسه متصل بالحدقة وقاعدته سطح المرئي، حتى أنه وتر لزاوية المخروط، ومعلوم أن وتر الزاوية كلما قرب من الزاوية، كان الساق أصغر، والزاوية أعظم - وكلما بعد فبالعكس، والشبح الذي في الزاوية الكبرى، أعظم من الذي في الصغرى، وهذا إنما يستقيم إذا جعلنا موضع الإبصار هو الزاوية على ما هو رأي الانطباع، لا القاعدة على ما هو رأي خروج الشعاع فإنها لا تتفاوت . ورد بأن لا نسلم أنه لا سبب سوى ذلك. كيف وأصحاب الشعاع أيضاً يثبتون سببه، على أن استلزام عظم زاوية الرؤية عظم المرئي وصغرها صغره محل نظر.
وإلى ما ذكرنا من وجوه الرد أشار بقوله وهو ضعيف .
The first group adhered to [the impression theory] by [presenting] aspects:
One of them—and it is the chief one—is that the eye is a polished, luminous body, and every such body, when it faces an opaque colored object, its image is imprinted in it like a mirror. As for the major premise, it is obvious; as for the minor premise, [it is supported] by the light seen in darkness when one who awakens from sleep rubs his eye, and likewise when passing the hand over the back of a black cat, and because when a person looks toward his nose he may see a circle of brightness upon it, and when he awakens from sleep he may see what is near him for a time then lose it, and that is because the eye is filled with light at that time, and when he closes one eye, the pupil of the other eye dilates—and that is only because a luminous substance fills it, and because if the luminous spirits did not flow from the brain to the eye, the two openings of sight would not have been made hollow. But after its completion, this only proves the imprinting of the image, not that sight occurs by means of it.
A second [argument]: that the other senses only perceive by the image of the sensible coming to them, not by something exiting from them to the sensible, so likewise [for] sight. It is refuted by [saying] it is an analogy without common ground.
A third [argument]: that whoever looks at the sun for a long time then turns away, its image remains in his eye for a time. It is refuted by [saying] the image is in his imagination, not his eye, as when he closes the eye.
A fourth [argument]: that the very same thing, when near to the observer, is seen larger than when far away—and that is only because the imprint is on a cone of transparent air, its apex connected to the pupil and its base to the surface of the visible object, such that it is the chord of the angle of the cone, and it is known that the chord of the angle, the closer to the angle, the smaller the leg and the larger the angle—and the farther, the opposite, and the image at the larger angle is larger than that at the smaller. This only holds if we make the locus of sight the angle, as is the opinion of the impression [theory], not the base, as is the opinion of the emission of the ray, for it does not vary. It is refuted by [saying] we do not concede that there is no cause other than that; how [can that be] when the proponents of the ray also establish its cause, and indeed the implication that the largeness of the visual angle entails the largeness of the visible [object] and its smallness entails its smallness is a matter of scrutiny.
And to what we have mentioned of the refutations, he alluded by his saying: 'and it is weak.'
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes four arguments advanced by proponents of the impression theory (al-intiba') of vision. The author begins by stating that the first group (the impressionists) adhere to their theory by presenting several 'aspects' (wujuh), i.e., arguments.

**First argument (the chief one):** The eye is a polished, luminous body. Whenever such a body faces an opaque colored object, the object's image is imprinted in it, like a mirror. The major premise (every polished luminous body does this) is obvious. The minor premise (the eye is such a body) is supported by empirical observations: light seen when rubbing the eyes after sleep, a circle of brightness seen when looking toward the nose, dilation of the pupil, and the flow of luminous spirits from brain to eye. However, the author notes after summarizing this argument that it only proves the imprinting of an image, not that sight occurs by means of that imprint.

**Second argument:** By analogy with the other senses, which perceive by receiving the image of the sensible, sight must also work by reception. The refutation: this is an analogy (tamthil) without a common ground (jami'), meaning the analogy is invalid.

**Third argument:** Afterimages: when looking at the sun and turning away, the image remains in the eye. The refutation: the image is in the imagination (khayal), not the eye, as when one closes the eye and still sees an afterimage.

**Fourth argument:** The same object appears larger when near and smaller when far, explained by the geometry of a cone: the image is imprinted on a cone of transparent air, and the visual angle determines size. This only works if the locus of sight is the apex (as impression theory holds), not if it is the base (as in ray theory). The refutation: there may be other causes, and the claimed necessity that angle size determines apparent size is open to scrutiny.

The author concludes by saying that he has alluded to the refutations and that the impression theory is weak (da'if).

Key Terms
المخروط (al-makhrut)
الزاوية (al-zawiya)
الوتر (al-watar)
الحدقة (al-hadaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The geometric cone argument proves the impression theory definitively.
Correction: Al-Taftazani points out that other explanations exist (e.g., ray theory) and that the necessary connection between angle size and apparent size is not established.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Taftazani reject the geometric cone argument?
A) The cone geometry is incorrect.
B) There may be other causes for size change, and the link between angle size and apparent size is open to scrutiny.
C) The eye is not a cone.
D) The argument only works for near objects.

Key Takeaway
The geometric argument for impression theory is not definitive because other models can also account for size perception, and the causal link is uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Passage 281 states that the causal link between visual angle and perceived size is 'a matter of scrutiny'. What deeply held belief about your own life—perhaps about how you see yourself or others—have you accepted as obvious without ever subjecting it to the same scrutiny? Are you willing to question that belief today, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
Al-Taftazani refutes the geometric argument by pointing out that other explanations exist. In your spiritual life, have you ever assumed that a particular practice or understanding was the only correct path, ignoring alternative valid perspectives? How might your relationship with Allah deepen if you humbly examined those assumptions?
?
The same object appears larger when near and smaller when far, but the cause is not simple. Consider a problem or worry that feels overwhelming right now. Could it be that your emotional 'nearness' makes it seem larger than it truly is? What step can you take today to gain distance and see it more accurately in light of this lesson's caution about overreliance on geometry?

Four Arguments for the Ray Theory of Vision
Al-Taftazani presents four arguments for the ray theory of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments presented by proponents of the ray theory of vision and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The ray theory is supported by variations in visual acuity, pathological cases, and the geometry of sight.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents four arguments advanced by those who believe vision occurs via an emitted ray from the eye. These arguments rely on empirical observations and analogies. As you read, note how each argument builds a case for the existence of the visual ray, though the fourth remains incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the structure: four separate arguments.
The passage ends mid-fourth argument; we only have the beginning of it.
2
Identify each argument's logic
For each of the first three complete arguments, identify the observation and how it supports the ray theory. The fourth is incomplete but you can infer its direction.
3
Compare with the impression theory
Notice that the first argument explicitly contrasts with the impression theory (law kāna al-ibṣāru bi-l-inṭibāʿ...). Consider why the ray theory might explain the variations better.
4
Reflect on dialectical context
Remember that these arguments are presented as part of a debate. They are not necessarily the author's own view but are being examined.
The common misunderstanding is that these are definitive proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك القائلون بالشعاع أيضاً بوجوه :
أحدها: أن من قل شعاع بصره، كان إدراكه للقريب أصح من إدراكه للبعيد، لتفرق الشعاع في البعيد، ومن كثر شعاع بصره مع غلظه، كان إدراكه للبعيد أصح، لأن الحركة في المسافة الطويلة تفيده رقة وصفاء، ولو كان الإبصار بالانطباع لما تفاوت الحال .
وثانيها: أن الأجهر يبصر بالليل دون النهار، لأن شعاع بصره لقلته يتحلل نهاراً بشعاع الشمس، فلا يبصر ويجتمع ليلاً فيقوى على الإبصار، والأعشى بالعكس، لأن شعاع بصره لغلظه لا يقوي على الإبصار، إلا إذا أفادته الشمس رقة وصفاء .
وثالثها: أن الإنسان إذا نظر إلى ورقة قرأها كلها لم يظهر له منها إلا السطر الذي يحدق نحوه البصر، وما ذاك إلا بسبب أن مسقط أن مسقط سهم مخروط الشعاع أصح إدراكاً من جوانبه .
ورابعها : أن الإنسان يرى في الظلمة كأن نوراً انفصل من عينه، وأشرق على
The proponents of the ray [theory] also adhered by [presenting] aspects:
One of them: that one whose visual ray is weak, his perception of the near is more correct than his perception of the far, because the ray disperses in the far; and one whose visual ray is strong along with its coarseness, his perception of the far is more correct, because movement over a long distance gives it fineness and clarity. And if sight were by impression, the situation would not differ.
A second [argument]: that the photophobic (ajhar) sees at night but not in the daytime, because his visual ray, due to its paucity, dissolves in the daytime by the ray of the sun, so he does not see, and it gathers at night so he becomes strong enough to see; and the night-blind (a'sha) is the opposite, because his visual ray, due to its coarseness, is not strong enough for sight unless the sun gives it fineness and clarity.
A third [argument]: that when a person looks at a page reading it entirely, nothing appears to him from it except the line at which he directs his gaze, and that is only because the projection of the arrow of the cone of the ray is more accurate in perception than its sides.
A fourth [argument]: that a person sees in darkness as if a light separated from his eye and shone upon
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments advanced by proponents of the ray theory (al-shu'a') of vision. Unlike the previous lesson, these arguments are given without explicit refutation in the text window (the text ends mid-fourth argument). The author presents them as support for the ray theory.

**First argument:** Variation in visual acuity depending on the strength of the visual ray. Someone with weak rays sees near objects better because the ray scatters over distance. Someone with thick, strong rays sees far objects better because the long journey refines the ray. If vision were by impression (intiba'), there would be no such variation. This argument uses the observed fact that distant vision varies with eye health to support the idea of an emitted ray.

**Second argument:** The photophobic (ajhar) can see at night but not in daylight because his weak ray is overwhelmed by the sun's rays. The night-blind (a'sha) is the opposite: his thick ray needs the sun to refine it. This again uses pathological cases to argue for an emitted ray that interacts with external light.

**Third argument:** When reading a page, only the line directly looked at (the 'aiming' line) is clearly seen, while others are blurred. This is attributed to the 'arrow' (sahm) of the cone of the ray hitting the line most accurately, while sides are less accurate. This suggests a conical structure of emitted rays.

**Fourth argument (incomplete):** In darkness, one seems to see a light emanating from the eye. This observation is taken as direct evidence for the existence of a visual ray. The text cuts off before completing this argument.

Key Terms
الشعاع
شعاع البصر
الأجهر
الأعشى
مخروط الشعاع
سهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are presented as definitive proofs.
Correction: They are part of a dialectical presentation; the author may later refute them.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument for the ray theory, why does the photophobic (ajhar) see at night but not during the day?
A) The sun's rays strengthen his own ray.
B) His ray is weak and dissolves in sunlight.
C) His ray is thick and needs darkness to become fine.
D) The impression theory explains it better.

Key Takeaway
The four arguments for the ray theory rely on empirical observations of visual variation, pathological cases, and geometric accuracy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ajhar sees at night but not in the day, and the a‘shā sees in the day but not at night. In what areas of your spiritual life are you like the ajhar—shunning the full light of truth, comfortable only in partial darkness? What would it take for you to step into the full sun of divine guidance without your 'ray' dissolving?
?
The third argument shows that when you read a page, only the line you fixate on is clear; the sides are blurry. Are there aspects of your faith—or of your own soul—that you keep blurry because you are fixated on a single line? What would happen if you turned your gaze toward the margins you have been avoiding?

Optical Illusions and the Soul's Interpretation of Light Rays
Explains optical illusions like double vision and inverted images using ray theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic scholars explained optical illusions such as double vision, mirror images, and inverted reflections using the theory of ray propagation and the soul's habitual interpretation.

Core Idea
Illusions are not errors of the eye, but of the soul's habitual reading of reflected rays as straight.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explores several common optical illusions and their causes within the framework of ray optics. The author demonstrates that phenomena like double vision, mirror images, and the inverted reflection of trees in water result from the way rays reflect off surfaces and how the soul, accustomed to straight-line vision, interprets these reflections. The science of optics (ʿilm al-manāẓir) is referenced as the authoritative source for detailed proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the range of illusions described: squinting/double vision, moon in water, mirror, inverted tree.
The passage uses technical terms like bāṣira (visual faculty) and shiʿāʿ (ray).
2
Identify the common principle
All illusions involve reflection of rays. The soul habitually interprets rays as straight, leading to misperception when rays are bent or reflected.
The key phrase: 'al-nafs lā tudrik al-inʿikās li-taʿawwudihā...'
3
Focus on the inverted tree example
This is the most detailed example. Trace the ray path: from eye to water surface, reflecting to tree top and base at different distances. The soul assumes the ray continues straight into water, so it sees the top as more submerged.
The inversion arises because the reflected ray from the top comes from a nearer point than the bottom.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters in kalam: it shows that sensory perception is not always reliable and requires rational analysis. The reference to 'ilm al-manāẓir indicates a systematic science behind these phenomena.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخالفة الأحول، وفيما إذا وضعنا السبابة والوسطى على العين مع الاختلاف في الوضع، ونظرنا إلى السراج فإنا نراه اثنين وكذا إذا نظرنا إلى الماء عند طلوع القمر، فإنا نرى في الماء قمراً بالشعاع النافذ فيه ، وفي السماء قمراً بالشعاع المنعكس من سطح الماء إلى السماء ، ومن هذا القبيل رؤية الشيء في المرآة، وذلك أن الشعاع الممتد من الباصرة إلى الجسم الصقيل ينعكس منه إلى جسم آخر، وضعه من ذلك الصقيل كوضع الباصرة منه ، بشرط أن تكون جهته لجهة الرائي. وأما السبب في رؤية الشجر على شط النهر منتكساً، فهو أن الشعاع إذا وقع على سطح الماء ينعكس منه إلى رأس الشجر من موضع أقرب إلى الرائي، وإلى أسفله من موضع أبعد من الرائي إلى أن تتصل قاعدة الشجرة بقاعدة عكسه، والنفس لا تدرك الانعكاس لتعودها برؤية الأشياء على استقامة الشعاع ، فتحسب الشعاع المنعكس نافذاً في الماء، فترى رأس الشجر أكثر نزولاً في الماء لكونه أبعد منه وباقي أجزائه على الترتيب إلى قاعدة الشجر، فيرى منتكساً، وبيان ذلك بالتحقيق في علم المناظر.
The squinting person's opposition, and when we place the index and middle fingers on the eye with difference in position, and look at a lamp, we see it as two. Likewise, when we look at water at moonrise, we see in the water a moon by the ray penetrating it, and in the sky a moon by the ray reflected from the surface of the water to the sky. Of this kind is seeing an object in a mirror: that the ray extending from the visual faculty (al-bāṣira) to the polished body (al-ṣaqīl) is reflected from it to another object, whose position relative to that polished body is like the position of the visual faculty relative to it, provided that its direction is toward the viewer. As for the cause of seeing trees on the riverbank inverted, it is that when the ray falls on the surface of the water, it is reflected from it to the top of the tree from a place closer to the viewer, and to its bottom from a place farther from the viewer, until the base of the tree connects with the base of its inversion. The soul (al-nafs) does not perceive the reflection due to its habit of seeing objects along the straight path of the ray, so it supposes the reflected ray to be penetrating into the water, and thus sees the top of the tree as more immersed in the water because it is farther from it, and the rest of its parts in order down to the base of the tree, so it is seen inverted. The detailed explanation of that is in the science of optics (ʿilm al-manāẓir).
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains several optical illusions and their causes using the theory of ray optics. It begins with the case of a squinting person or placing fingers on the eye to see a lamp as two, illustrating double vision due to different alignments. Next, it describes seeing two moons at moonrise: one in the water via a direct ray, and one in the sky via a ray reflected from the water's surface. Similarly, seeing an object in a mirror involves a ray from the eye to the polished surface, then reflecting to another object. The main focus is explaining why trees on a riverbank appear inverted. The ray falls on the water surface, reflects to the top of the tree from a nearer point and to the bottom from a farther point. The soul, accustomed to straight rays, interprets the reflected ray as straight, so it perceives the top as more submerged, producing an inverted image. The passage concludes by referring to the science of optics for detailed proof.

Key Terms
باصرة
شعاع
انعكاس
نفس
علم المناظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Optical illusions are caused by errors in the eye's structure.
Correction: The author argues that illusions arise because the soul interprets reflected rays as straight due to habit, not because the eye is flawed.

Checkpoint
Why does the soul perceive the reflected ray as straight when seeing an inverted tree?
A) Because the ray is actually straight
B) Because of the soul's habit of seeing objects along straight rays
C) Because the water surface is curved
D) Because the tree is closer to the viewer

Key Takeaway
Common optical illusions are explained by the reflection of rays and the soul's habitual perception of straight rays, rather than by defects in the eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The soul did not perceive the reflection because of its habit of seeing along straight rays. Commit to one specific area where you will seek the 'straight ray' of truth from revelation rather than your accustomed perception.
?

?
Consider the squinting person's double vision: a small misalignment distorts perception. What small misalignments in your heart (like a habit or bias) might be causing you to see truth as double or distorted? What step can you take today to align your vision with the Qur'an and Sunnah?
