The Common Sense: Definition and Evidence
Defines the common sense and provides evidence for its existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and the evidence for its existence as an internal faculty that collects sensory images.

Core Idea
The common sense is the internal faculty where external sensory images converge.

In classical Islamic psychology, the common sense is one of the internal senses that unifies perceptions from the five external senses. Al-Sanusi defines it here and supports its existence with three clear pieces of evidence from everyday experience and special cases. Understanding this faculty helps explain how we perceive wholes, such as a moving flame as a circle, and how sleepers can see things not present.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the technical terms like 'ḥiss mushtarak' and 'ṣuwar al-maḥsūsāt'.
The passage is dense with information; take your time.
2
Identify the definition
Find how the common sense is defined: 'the faculty in which the images of sensed objects are collected'.
This definition focuses on its role as a gathering place.
3
Examine the three pieces of evidence
List the three evidences: (1) judgment comparing sensed objects, (2) perception during sleep/sickness, (3) seeing a falling drop as a line and a flame as a circle.
Each evidence shows the need for an internal faculty beyond the external senses.
4
Understand the underlying principles
Note the two principles: sensory images are not imprinted in the soul, and sight only receives facing objects.
These principles support why the common sense is necessary.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Realize that the common sense is distinct from the rational soul (nafs), which judges but does not directly receive images.
This clarifies the soul's role as perceiver and judge, not receptacle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما الحواس الباطنة) فمنها الحس المشترك، وهي القوة التي يجتمع فيها صور المحسوسات بتأديها إليها من طرق الحواس، يدل عليها الحكم ببعض المحسوسات على البعض ومشاهدة النائم والمريض ما ليس في الخارج، ومشاهدة الكل القطرة النازلة خطاً، والشعلة الجوالة دائرة، ومبناه على أن صور المحسوسات لا ترتسم في النفس، وإن كانت هي الحاكمة والمدركة، وعلى ضرورة أنه لا يرتسم في البصر إلا المقابل، أو ما هو في حكمه .
He said: 'As for the internal senses (al-ḥawāss al-bāṭina):' among them is the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), which is the faculty in which the images of sensed objects (ṣuwar al-maḥsūsāt) are collected, as they are conveyed to it from the pathways of the [external] senses. Evidence for it is the judgment concerning some sensed objects by reference to others, and the observation of the sleeping person and the sick person of what is not externally present, and everyone's observation of the falling drop as a line, and the moving flame as a circle. And its basis is that the images of sensed objects are not imprinted in the soul (al-nafs), even though it is the judge and perceiver, and upon the necessity that nothing is imprinted in the sight except the facing object or what is in its ruling.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defines one of the internal senses: the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak). It is described as the faculty where images from the five external senses are gathered. Evidence for its existence includes: (1) we make judgments comparing different sensed objects, which requires a common locus; (2) sleeping and sick people perceive things not present, so a non-external faculty must store images; (3) everyone observes a falling drop as a continuous line and a moving flame as a circle, indicating a composite image formed over time. The underlying principle is that sensory images are not imprinted directly in the soul (the rational self), but in a separate faculty, and that only facing objects imprint on the eye, so non-facing objects require an internal faculty to combine them.

Key Terms
حواس باطنة
حس مشترك
صور المحسوسات
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul (nafs) directly receives sensory images and stores them.
Correction: The soul judges and perceives, but sensory images are imprinted in the common sense, not in the soul itself.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is presented as evidence for the common sense?
A) The ability to see distant stars
B) The observation of a falling drop as a line
C) The ability to taste food
D) The sense of touch

Key Takeaway
The common sense is the internal faculty that collects sensory images, evidenced by cross-sensory judgments, perception during sleep, and perception of motion as continuous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage tells you that your perception of a moving flame or a falling drop is not a simple external fact but a construction by an internal faculty. How does this truth shake your trust in the raw data of your senses? When was the last time you assumed your eyes showed you exactly what is there, without considering the internal processing behind it?
?
You have seen a dream that felt utterly real. This lesson says that the same faculty that stores images during wakefulness also presents them in sleep. Given that, how do you distinguish between a perception that reflects external reality and one that is merely a construction of your common sense? What is the ultimate criterion?
?
The author emphasizes that the soul (nafs) judges and perceives but does not receive images directly. This means your 'self' is not the same as your sensory experiences. Are you living as though your identity is wrapped up in what you sense? What would it mean to identify more with the soul that judges rather than the images that come and go?

Memory and Its Distinction from Imagination
Sanusi defines memory and distinguishes it from imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the memory faculty (al-hafiza) and how it differs from imagination (al-khayal) in Islamic psychology.

Core Idea
Memory preserves meanings; imagination preserves images—they are distinct powers.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces another internal sense: the faculty of memory. He clarifies its function as preserving the judgments of the estimative faculty and distinguishes it from imagination. This distinction is crucial for understanding how the soul processes and retains information.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the key terms: الحافظة, الوهم, الخيال, الحس المشترك.
The passage is short but packed with comparisons.
2
Identify the function of memory
Note that al-hafiza preserves the judgments of al-wahm (the estimative faculty). It is also called al-dhakira when retrieving.
3
Contrast memory and imagination
Understand that memory preserves meanings, while imagination preserves images. The author explains that reception and preservation are different powers.
This is the core distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها الحافظة) لأحكام الوهم وتسمى الذاكرة باعتبار استرجاعها. هي للوهم كالخيال للحس المشترك، ووجه تغايرها أن قوة القبول غير قوة الحفظ، والحافظة للمعاني غير الحافظ للصور، ويسميها قوم ذاكرة، إذ بها الذكر أعني ملاحظة المحفوظ بعد الذهول عنه، ومتذكرة إذ بها التذكر. أعني الاحتيال لاستعراض الصور بعد ما اندرست.
He said: (And from among them is the Preserving faculty) for the judgments of the estimative faculty, and it is called the memory by virtue of its retrieval. It is for the estimative faculty like the imagination is for the common sense. The reason for their difference is that the power of reception is not the power of preservation, and the preservative of meanings is not the preserver of images. Some people call it 'rememberer', because by it is remembrance, i.e., observation of what is preserved after forgetfulness of it; and 're-collector' because by it is recollection, i.e., trying to retrieve images after they have faded.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines the internal sense of memory (al-hafiza). The author states that it preserves the judgments of the estimative faculty (al-wahm) and is called al-dhakira when retrieving. He then compares it to imagination (al-khayal) in relation to common sense: as imagination preserves images, so memory preserves meanings. The distinction is that reception and preservation are different powers; one faculty receives, another preserves. Memory preserves meanings, while imagination preserves images. Some call it 'rememberer' (dhakira) because by it one observes what is preserved after being heedless, and 're-collector' (mutadhakkira) because by it one actively retrieves faded images.

Key Terms
الحافظة
الذاكرة
الوهم
الخيال
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that memory and imagination are the same power
Correction: They are distinct: memory preserves meanings, while imagination preserves images.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between memory (al-hafiza) and the estimative faculty (al-wahm)?
A) Memory preserves the judgments of the estimative faculty.
B) Memory operates independently of the estimative faculty.
C) Memory is identical to the imagination.
D) Memory only preserves images.

Key Takeaway
Memory preserves the judgments of the estimative faculty and is distinct from imagination in that it preserves meanings while imagination preserves images.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your memory preserves meaningful judgments about the world and about your Creator. When you experience a moment of clarity about Allah's greatness, do you actively work to preserve that meaning in your heart, or do you let it slip away like a fading image? What concrete step will you take today to etch one such truth into your memory?
?
The passage distinguishes between mere preservation (al-hifz) and active recollection (al-tadhakkur) after forgetfulness. Think of a spiritual truth you once knew deeply but have become neglectful of. Will you make time today to retrieve and re-engage with it, or will you let it remain forgotten?
?
Sanusi says memory is for meanings, not images. Your mind is constantly filled with images—social media, entertainment, distractions. But what truly meaningful judgments are you storing? Is the content of your memory aligned with what will benefit you in the Hereafter, or is it filled with fleeting images that leave no mark on your soul? Commit to memorizing one meaningful phrase (like an attribute of Allah) today and repeat it until it is preserved.

The Compositive Imagination and Its Two Modes
Sanusi describes the compositive imagination and its two modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the compositive imagination (al-mutasarifa) and how it functions in two modes: as the imaginative faculty (mutakhayyila) when directed by the estimative faculty, and as the thinking faculty (mufakkira) when directed by the intellect.

Core Idea
The compositive imagination is a neutral tool; its mode depends on which higher faculty directs it.

Sanusi now turns to the inner cognitive faculty known as the compositive imagination. This power does not operate on its own but is directed by either the estimative faculty or the intellect. Understanding its two modes helps clarify how humans combine images and meanings in both dreaming and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify key terms
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the terms al-mutasarifa, al-mutakhayyila, al-mufakkira, and al-hadd al-awsat (middle term).
These terms are central to the discussion.
2
Understand the two names based on use
The same faculty is called mutakhayyila when used by the estimative faculty, and mufakkira when used by the intellect. Note the examples given.
The faculty itself is the same; the name changes with the directing power.
3
Examine the examples of composition and division
The passage gives examples like imagining a human with two heads (composition) or without a head (division). Also, imagining an enemy as a friend (division of meaning).
These show how the faculty works on images and meanings.
4
Recognize its constant activity and role in reasoning
The faculty is always restless, never sleeping or waking. It is used to hunt the middle term in syllogisms by surveying the memory.
This highlights its importance in logical reasoning.
5
Learn how the soul directs it
The soul can use the faculty either via the estimative alone (mutakhayyila) or via the intellect alone or with the estimative (mufakkira). The mode depends on the directive force.
This shows the compositive imagination is a tool under higher control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها المتصرفة) الصور والمعاني بالتركيب والتفصيل وتسمى باعتبار استعمال العقل إياها مفكرة والوهم متخيلة. أي في الصور المأخوذة عن الحس والمعاني المدركة بالوهم بتركيب بعضها مع بعض، وتفصيل بعضها عن بعض كتصور إنسان له رأسان، أو لا رأس له، وتصور العدو صديقاً، وبالعكس، وهي دائماً لا تسكن نوماً ولا يقظة، وبها يقتنص الحد الأوسط، باستعراض ما في الحافظة، وهي المحاكية للمدركات والهيئات المزاجية، وينتقل إلى الضد والشبيه، وليس من شأنها أن يكون عملها منتظماً، بل النفس هي التي تستعملها على أي نظام تريد إما بواسطة القوة الوهمية من غير تصرف عقلي، وحينئذ تسمى متخيلة، أو بواسطة القوة العقلية وحدها، أو مع الوهمية. وحينئذ تسمى مفكرة.
He said: (And from among them is the Disposing faculty) of images and meanings by composition and division, and it is called, by virtue of the intellect using it, the 'mufakkira' (thinking faculty), and by virtue of the estimative faculty using it, the 'mutakhayyila' (imaginative). That is, in the images taken from sense and the meanings perceived by the estimative faculty, by combining some with others and separating some from others, such as conceiving a human with two heads or without a head, and conceiving an enemy as a friend and vice versa. It is always restless, neither sleeping nor waking. By it the middle term is hunted, by surveying what is in the memory. It imitates percepts and temperamental states, and transitions to opposite and similar. It is not its nature to have its work ordered; rather, the soul uses it on whatever order it wills, either by means of the estimative faculty without intellectual intervention - then it is called mutakhayyila - or by means of the intellectual faculty alone or with the estimative - then it is called mufakkira.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines the compositive imagination (al-mutasarifa). It operates on images and meanings by combining and separating them. When used by the intellect, it is called mufakkira (thinking); when used by the estimative faculty, it is called mutakhayyila (imaginative). The author gives examples: imagining a human with two heads, or an enemy as a friend. This faculty is constantly active, never resting. Its role in reasoning is to hunt for the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) by surveying the memory. It imitates percepts and moods, moves to opposites and similars. It is not inherently ordered; the soul directs it either through the estimative (without intellectual intervention, resulting in mutakhayyila) or through the rational faculty alone or with the estimative (resulting in mufakkira).

Key Terms
المتصرفة
المفكرة
المتخيلة
الحد الأوسط
الوهم
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the compositive imagination operates independently, without direction from other faculties.
Correction: The passage states that the soul uses it on whatever order it wills, either via the estimative or intellectual faculty. Its work is not naturally ordered; it is directed.

Checkpoint
When the compositive imagination is used by the intellect alone or with the estimative faculty, what is it called?
A) Mutakhayyila
B) Mufakkira
C) Hafiza
D) Khayal

Key Takeaway
The compositive imagination adapts to the directing faculty, functioning as either imaginative or thinking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your compositive imagination never rests, even in sleep. When your intellect is not directing it, what kind of images and associations arise in your mind? Are you aware of how often your imagination composes and divides without your conscious choice? Who is truly directing your inner mental world right now?
?
The passage says the soul uses this faculty 'on whatever order it wills.' When you imagine an enemy as a friend or vice versa, are you employing intellectual deliberation (mufakkira) or mere estimation (mutakhayyila)? Have you ever mistaken your own imaginative construction for reality? How can you train your soul to invoke the mufakkira more than the mutakhayyila?
?
Your memory is constantly surveyed by the compositive imagination to hunt the middle term. When seeking truth, do you let your intellect guide that search, or do you allow random associations from estimation to dominate? What would change if you consciously directed your imaging faculty toward rational inquiry?

Traditional Localization of Internal Senses
Sanusi enumerates the five internal senses and states the basis for their multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional medieval theory that the five internal senses are localized in specific regions of the brain and the logical basis for this theory.

Core Idea
Each internal sense has a specific brain location, supported by the principle that different effects require different causes.

Sanusi opens this section by stating the standard view of his time: the five internal senses are each assigned to a distinct part of the brain. He then explains why such a mapping is necessary, appealing to the diversity of sensory functions and the principle that a single faculty cannot produce multiple distinct effects. This passage lays out the classical position that he will later critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on standard localization
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the five senses and their assigned brain regions. Compare with the translation.
The senses are: common sense, imagination, compositive imagination, estimative, and memory.
2
Identify the logical basis
Notice the phrase 'العمدة في تعدد هذه القوى...' which provides the rationale: different effects arise from different faculties, and these faculties must be located in different places.
The author argues that if all were one, the effects would not differ.
3
Reflect on the principle of unity
The argument assumes that a single cause cannot produce diverse effects without differentiation. This is a key principle in kalam and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (خاتمة) مقدم البطن الأول من الدماغ، محل للحس المشترك، ومؤخره للخيال، والأوسط للمتخيلة، ومقدم الآخر للوهم، وآخره للحافظة، والعمدة في تعدد هذه القوى وتعيين محالها، تعدد الآثار، واختلافها باختلاف المحال مع القطع، بأن الإحساس إنما هو للقوى الجسمانية، وأنه لا معنى لآلتها إلا ما هو محل لها، وأنها لا تخيل بعضو آخر، فلا يصح اتحادها، وعود الكثرة والاختلال إلى آلاتها)، والكل ضعيف. مما علم بالتشريح أن للدماغ تجاويف ثلاثة أعظمها البطن الأول، وأصغرها البطن الأوسط، وهو كمنفذ من البطن المقدم إلى البطن المؤخر. وقد دل اختلال الحس المشترك بآفة تعرض لمقدم البطن الأول من الدماغ دون غيره من أجزاء الدماغ، على أنه محله. وهكذا الدليل على كون الخيال في مؤخر البطن الأول، وكون المتخيلة في البطن الأوسط، وكون الوهم في مقدم البطن الأخير، وكون الحافظة في آخره. وأما الدليل على تعدد هذه القوى، فهو اختلاف الاثار مع ما تقرر عندهم من أن الواحد، لا يكون مبدأ للكثير.
He said: (Conclusion) The front of the first ventricle of the brain is the place of common sense, its back is for imagination, the middle is for the compositive faculty, the front of the other ventricle is for the estimative faculty, and its back is for the memory. The basis for the multiplicity of these powers and the determination of their places is the multiplicity of effects and their difference according to difference of places, along with the certainty that sensation belongs only to corporeal powers, and that there is no meaning for its instrument except that it is a place for it, and that they are not imagined by another organ, so their unity is not valid, and the multiplicity and disorder revert to their instruments.) And all of this is weak. It is known from dissection that the brain has three cavities, the largest being the first ventricle, the smallest the middle ventricle, which is like a passage from the front ventricle to the back. The disorder of common sense due to an affliction affecting the front of the first ventricle of the brain to the exclusion of other parts of the brain indicates that it is its place. Likewise for the proof that imagination is in the back of the first ventricle, the compositive in the middle ventricle, the estimative in the front of the last ventricle, and the memory in its back. As for the proof for the multiplicity of these powers, it is the difference of effects along with what is established with them that the one cannot be the source of many.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the khatima (conclusion) on the localization of the five internal senses. The author states the standard view: common sense in front of first ventricle, imagination in back of first, compositive in middle, estimative in front of second ventricle, memory in back of second. The basis is that different effects require different faculties, and since effects differ by place, the faculties must be multiple. The author then notes that 'all of this is weak' — likely a critical comment. He then provides evidence from dissection: lesions in specific parts cause specific dysfunctions. Finally, the proof for multiplicity is the principle that one thing (al-wahid) cannot be the source of many effects (al-kathir).

Key Terms
البطن الأول
الحس المشترك
الخيال
المتخيلة
الوهم
الحافظة
الآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the five internal senses are simply mental concepts without physical location.
Correction: The medieval tradition held that each internal sense is a corporeal faculty located in a specific part of the brain.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which internal sense is located in the front of the first ventricle?
A) Imagination
B) Common sense
C) Estemative
D) Memory

Key Takeaway
The traditional theory assigns each internal sense a specific brain region, justified by the principle that different effects require different faculties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a physical brain, and this passage tells you that your imagination, memory, and even your ability to perceive hidden meanings have specific locations. Does that make your inner life feel more fragile or more real? How do you react to the idea that your thoughts are tied to matter?
?
Sanusi presents this theory as a logical deduction from observable effects. What effects in your own life—habits, recurring thoughts, memories—might point to the existence of faculties you haven't consciously acknowledged? Will you take time today to examine one such effect?
?
If even a respected scholar like Sanusi later calls this theory weak, how much trust should you place in the 'certainties' you currently hold about your own mind? Are you willing to let your assumptions be challenged, even uncomfortable ones?

Critique and Anatomical Evidence for Localization
Sanusi critiques the traditional theory and presents anatomical evidence from brain lesions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's critique of the traditional localization theory and the empirical evidence from brain lesions that he offers as support.

Core Idea
The traditional localization is weak; empirical evidence from dissection shows that lesions in specific brain areas cause specific deficits.

After presenting the standard theory, Sanusi immediately dismisses it as weak ('والكل ضعيف'). He then provides what he considers stronger evidence: observations from dissection (تشريح) that damage to particular parts of the brain leads to dysfunction of specific senses. He also reiterates the logical principle that the one cannot be the source of many. This lesson examines his critique and the anatomical evidence he marshals.

Guided Path
1
Read the critical passage
Start with 'والكل ضعيف' — understand that the author is rejecting the entire previous theory. Then read the rest of the passage.
The author does not completely discard localization, but he questions the traditional mapping.
2
Examine the anatomical evidence
Sanusi cites dissection findings: the brain has three ventricles, and lesions in specific parts affect specific senses. Identify each correspondence.
For example, lesion in front of first ventricle impairs common sense.
3
Understand the concluding proof
The final sentence states the proof for multiplicity: difference of effects and the principle that one cannot be the source of many. See how this supports localization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (خاتمة) مقدم البطن الأول من الدماغ، محل للحس المشترك، ومؤخره للخيال، والأوسط للمتخيلة، ومقدم الآخر للوهم، وآخره للحافظة، والعمدة في تعدد هذه القوى وتعيين محالها، تعدد الآثار، واختلافها باختلاف المحال مع القطع، بأن الإحساس إنما هو للقوى الجسمانية، وأنه لا معنى لآلتها إلا ما هو محل لها، وأنها لا تخيل بعضو آخر، فلا يصح اتحادها، وعود الكثرة والاختلال إلى آلاتها)، والكل ضعيف. مما علم بالتشريح أن للدماغ تجاويف ثلاثة أعظمها البطن الأول، وأصغرها البطن الأوسط، وهو كمنفذ من البطن المقدم إلى البطن المؤخر. وقد دل اختلال الحس المشترك بآفة تعرض لمقدم البطن الأول من الدماغ دون غيره من أجزاء الدماغ، على أنه محله. وهكذا الدليل على كون الخيال في مؤخر البطن الأول، وكون المتخيلة في البطن الأوسط، وكون الوهم في مقدم البطن الأخير، وكون الحافظة في آخره. وأما الدليل على تعدد هذه القوى، فهو اختلاف الاثار مع ما تقرر عندهم من أن الواحد، لا يكون مبدأ للكثير.
He said: (Conclusion) The front of the first ventricle of the brain is the place of common sense, its back is for imagination, the middle is for the compositive faculty, the front of the other ventricle is for the estimative faculty, and its back is for the memory. The disorder of common sense due to an affliction affecting the front of the first ventricle of the brain to the exclusion of other parts of the brain indicates that it is its place. Likewise for the proof that imagination is in the back of the first ventricle, the compositive in the middle ventricle, the estimative in the front of the last ventricle, and the memory in its back. As for the proof for the multiplicity of these powers, it is the difference of effects along with what is established with them that the one cannot be the source of many.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the khatima (conclusion) on the localization of the five internal senses. The author states the standard view: common sense in front of first ventricle, imagination in back of first, compositive in middle, estimative in front of second ventricle, memory in back of second. Why Physicians Limit Inner Faculties to Three
Explains why physicians restrict study to imagination, cogitation, and memory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practical medical reasons why classical physicians only recognized three inner faculties, and how these map to the brain's ventricles.

Core Idea
Physicians focus on health, not metaphysics — hence three faculties suffice.

In this passage, the author explains why physicians (al-atibba') restrict their study of inner faculties to just three: imagination, cogitation, and memory. Unlike philosophers or theologians who seek to classify every subtle power of the soul, physicians are only concerned with preserving bodily health and treating ailments. This practical orientation shapes their simplified model of the mind, which maps neatly onto the three ventricles of the brain.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the original Arabic text and the literal English translation. Notice the key terms for the three faculties and the brain regions.
The Arabic terms are: الخيال, المفكرة, الذاكرة.
2
Identify the physicians' reasoning
The passage states that physicians only need to preserve health and correct disorders, not to distinguish between types of faculties. Their knowledge is limited to actions and locations. Find this justification in the text.
Compare this to the broader philosophical or kalam approach that delves deeper into distinctions.
3
Map the faculties to brain ventricles
The author identifies three ventricles (anterior, middle, posterior) and assigns one or more faculties to each. Note that the front ventricle houses common sense and imagination; the middle ventricle houses cogitation (also called wahm); the posterior ventricle houses preservation and recollection (memory).
This mapping is central to understanding the medical model of the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (واقتصر الأطباء) : على الخيال والمفكرة والذاكرة] لما كان نظرهم مقصوراً على حفظ صحة القوى، وإصلاح اختلالها، ولم يحتاجوا إلى معرفة الفرق بين القوى، وتحقيق أنواعها، بل إلى معرفة أفعالها ومواضعها، وكانت الآفات العارضة لها، قد تتجانس اقتصروا على قوة في البطن المقدم من الدماغ سموها الحس المشترك والخيال وأخرى في البطن الأوسط سموها المفكرة وهي الوهم ، وأخرى في البطن المؤخر سموها الحافظة والمتذكرة .
He said: “And the physicians restricted [themselves] to imagination, cogitation, and memory.” Because their view was confined to preserving the health of the faculties and correcting their disorders, and they did not need to know the distinction between the faculties and the verification of their types, but rather to know their actions and locations. And since the accidental ailments affecting them might be similar, they restricted [themselves] to a faculty in the front ventricle of the brain, which they called common sense and imagination; another in the middle ventricle, which they called cogitation—and it is the estimative faculty; and another in the rear ventricle, which they called the preservative and recollective faculty.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage explains why physicians (al-atibba') only study three inner faculties: imagination (khayāl), cogitation (mufakkira), and memory (dhākira). The author clarifies that physicians are only concerned with preserving health and correcting illnesses, not with the metaphysical classification or verification of the types of faculties. They only need to know the actions and locations of the faculties. Since the ailments affecting these faculties are similar, they limit themselves to one faculty per brain ventricle. In the front ventricle is common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl). In the middle ventricle is cogitation (al-mufakkira), which is the estimative faculty (al-wahm). In the rear ventricle is the preservative (al-ḥāfiẓa) and recollective (al-mutadhakkira) faculty. The physicians' limitation is practical, not metaphysical.

Misconception: One might think that physicians provide a complete classification of all inner faculties.
Correction: Physicians only focus on three faculties relevant to health preservation, not a complete metaphysical classification.

Checkpoint

A) Because only three faculties truly exist.
B) Because they only need to preserve health and correct ailments, and the faculties' actions and locations suffice for that.
C) Because kalam only requires these three faculties.
D) Because these are the only faculties mentioned in revelation.

Key Takeaway
Physicians limit the inner faculties to three because their practical medical focus only requires knowledge of the actions and locations of faculties affecting health.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The physicians lumped common sense and imagination together. In your own life, have you ever oversimplified something complex about yourself just because it was 'enough' to get by? What might you need to re-examine more deeply starting today?

The Mover Faculties: Classification and Anatomy
Classifies mover faculties into inciting and active, with anatomical details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of mover faculties into inciting and active types, and the anatomical basis for muscle movement.

Core Idea
Mover faculties combine desire and physical action.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines the faculties that cause movement. It distinguishes between the inciting force of desire and the physical mechanism that executes movement. The text also provides a detailed anatomical description of muscle and nerve, grounding the abstract faculty in physical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the author's concise style in the matn.
2
Identify the two main categories
The passage divides mover faculties into inciting (shawqiyya) and active (fa‘ila). Find how each is described.
Inciting prompts movement toward benefit or away from harm.
3
Understand the subcategories of inciting
The inciting faculty is further split into appetitive (shahwiyya) and irascible (ghadabiyya). Note the triggers: perceived benefit vs. harm.
These correspond to desire and anger.
4
Study the active faculty's function
The active faculty works on muscles. It extends nerves away from the origin (for extension) or towards the origin (for contraction).
This explains how limbs move.
5
Examine the anatomical definitions
The passage defines muscle (compound of nerve, ligament, and flesh) and nerve (originating from brain/spinal cord). Understand their roles.
This ties the abstract faculty to physical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المحركة) فمنها شوقية باعثة على جذب ما يتصوره نافعاً، وتسمى شهوية، أو دفع ما يتصوره ضاراً، وتسمى غضبية، ومنها فاعلة لتمديد الأعصاب إلى جهة مبدأها كما في القبض، أو إلى خلاف جهته كما في البسط . لم يبسط الكلام في القوى المحركة بسطه في القوى المدركة، لأن المباحث الكلامية لا تتعلق بهذه تعلقها بتلك، والمراد بالمحركة أعم من الفاعلة للحركة، والباعثة عليها وتسمى شوقية ونزوعية، وتنقسم إلى شهوية وهي الباعثة على الحركة نحو ما يعتقد أو يظن نافعاً وغضبية وهي الباعثة على الحركة نحو ما يعتقد أو يظن ضاراً، وأما الفاعلة فهي قوة من شأنها أن تبسط العضل بإرخاء الأعصاب إلى خلاف جهة مبدأها لينبسط العضو المتحرك، أي يزداد طولاً، وينتقص عرضاً، أو تقبضه بتمديد الأعصاب إلى جهة مبدأها، لينقبض العضو المتحرك، أي يزداد عرضاً، وينتقص طولاً، والعضلة عضو مركب من العصب، ومن جسم شبيه بالعصب تنبت من أطراف العظام تسمى رباطاً وعقباً، ومن لحم احتشى به الفرج التي بين الأجزاء المنتفشة الحاصلة باشتباك العصب والرباط غشاء تخللها. والعصب جسم ينبت من الدماغ أو النخاع أبيض لدن لين في الانعطاف، صلب في الانفصال.
He said: “And as for the mover [faculties], among them is an inciting [faculty] that prompts the attraction of what it imagines as beneficial, and it is called appetitive; or the repulsion of what it imagines as harmful, and it is called irascible. And among them is an active [faculty] for extending the nerves towards its origin, as in contraction, or away from its origin, as in extension.” He did not elaborate on the mover faculties as he did on the perceptual faculties, because kalam investigations are not as concerned with these as with those. And what is meant by the mover is more general than the active [faculty] of movement and the inciter to it, and it is called inciting and desiring. It is divided into the appetitive, which prompts movement towards what is believed or thought to be beneficial, and the irascible, which prompts movement towards what is believed or thought to be harmful. As for the active [faculty], it is a power that expands the muscle by relaxing the nerves away from its origin so that the moving limb extends, i.e., increases in length and decreases in width; or contracts it by extending the nerves towards its origin so that the moving limb contracts, i.e., increases in width and decreases in length. The muscle is an organ composed of nerve, and a body resembling nerve that grows from the ends of bones called ligament and tendon, and flesh that fills the gaps between the swollen parts resulting from the intertwining of nerve and ligament, a membrane permeating them. The nerve is a body growing from the brain or spinal cord, white, soft, pliable in bending, tough in separation.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage classifies the mover faculties (al-quwā al-muḥarrika). The author first cites the matn stating that among them is an inciting (shawqiyya) faculty that prompts attraction of what is imagined as beneficial (appetitive/shahwiyya) or repulsion of what is imagined as harmful (irascible/ghaḍabiyya). There is also an active (fāʿila) faculty for extending nerves towards the origin (causing contraction) or away from the origin (causing extension). The commentary then explains that the author (al-Taftazani) did not elaborate on mover faculties as much as on perceptual faculties because kalam discussions are less concerned with these. The term 'mover' is more general than the active faculty; it includes both the inciter (shawqiyya/nuzū‘iyya) and the executor. The inciter is divided into appetitive (shahwiyya) and irascible (ghaḍabiyya) based on whether it seeks benefit or avoids harm. The active faculty is described in detail: it expands the muscle by relaxing nerves away from the origin, making the limb longer and thinner, or contracts it by extending nerves towards the origin, making the limb shorter and thicker. The passage then defines the muscle (ʿaḍala) as a composite organ made of nerve, a nerve-like body (ligament/tendon), and flesh, with a membrane; and defines nerve (ʿaṣab) as a white, pliable body originating from the brain or spinal cord.

العصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that movement is purely physical without any inciting desire.
Correction: The text shows that mover faculties include an inciting psychological component (shawqiyya) that prompts movement towards benefit or away from harm.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the function of the active (fāʿila) faculty?
A) It incites desire towards beneficial things.
B) It incites desire away from harmful things.
C) It expands or contracts the muscle by extending or relaxing nerves.
D) It is the same as the estimative faculty.

Key Takeaway
The mover faculties consist of an inciting psychological component and a physical active component that works through muscle and nerve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have an appetitive faculty that drives you toward what you think is beneficial. Right now, what are you chasing that you assume will bring you good? Are you sure it is truly beneficial in the sight of Allah, or have you been deceived by your own imagination? Will you stop today and ask for clarity before moving?
?
Your irascible faculty pushes you to repel what you perceive as harmful. When you last felt anger, did you let it move you without thought? Could it be that what you thought was harmful was actually a trial from Allah meant for your growth? Will you commit to pausing before acting in anger, and reflect on your perception?
?
Every voluntary movement you make—every step, every gesture—is carried out by your active faculty. Are you aware of the countless commands your soul sends to your limbs each day? How many of those movements are in obedience to Allah, and how many are heedless? Will you try today to bring intention into even your smallest actions?

The Principle of Desire and Loss of Faculties
Clarifies origin of desire and loss of faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the principle of desire originates from perceptual faculties (imagination or intellection), and that certain faculties may be absent in different beings by nature or accident.

Core Idea
Desire flows from perception, not from a separate mover faculty.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies a common confusion about the source of desire. While one might think desire arises from a special mover power, the author firmly places its origin in the perceptual faculties. Additionally, the text explains that not all faculties are present in every creature; some are missing naturally or accidentally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two main claims: one about desire, one about missing faculties.
The passage includes both the matn and Taftazani's commentary.
2
Analyze the correction about desire
The text first refutes the misconception that desire is a separate mover faculty. Identify how it argues that desire stems from perception (imagination or intellect).
Examples: animal longing, captive's desire, soul's desire for beauty.
3
Examine the discussion on missing faculties
The second part explains that perceptual and mover faculties can be absent by nature or accident. Note the examples: scorpion (no sight), moth (no imagination), congenital blindness, injury.
This shows that faculties are not universally present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما مبدأ الشوق) فمن القوى المدركة . قد يتوهم أن من القوى المحركة قوة أخرى هي مبدأ قريب للشوقية يعيد للفاعلة كالقوة التي ينبعث عنها شوق الالف بالشيء إلى مألوفه، وشوق المحبوس إلى خلاصه وشوق النفس إلى الفعل الجميل، فأشار إلى أن ذلك من قبيل القوى المدركة ، لأن مبدأ الشوق والنزوع تخيل أو تعقل . [قال(ثم بعض هذه القوى) قد يفقد في بعض أنواع الحيوان أو أشخاصه بحسب الخلقة أو العارض]. يعني المدركة والمحركة، وقد تفقد في بعض أنواع الحيوان كالبصر في العقرب ، والخيال في الفراشة أو أشخاصه بحسب الخلقة كالأكمه ، ومن ولد مفقود بعض الحواس أو الحركات أو بحسب العارض كمن أصابه آفة، أخلت ببعض إدراكاته أو حركاته .
He said: “And as for the principle of desire, it is from the perceptual faculties.” It might be imagined that among the mover faculties there is another power that is a proximate principle for the inciting [faculty], returning to the active [faculty], like the power from which arises the desire of a familiar [animal] for its familiar [place], the desire of a captive for his release, and the desire of the soul for beautiful action. So he indicated that that is of the kind of perceptual faculties, because the principle of desire and longing is imagination or intellection. [He said: “Then some of these faculties may be missing in some types of animals or their individuals, by nature or accident.”] He means the perceptual and mover [faculties]. They may be missing in some types of animals, like sight in the scorpion and imagination in the moth, or in their individuals by nature, like the blind [from birth], or one born missing some senses or movements, or by accident, like one afflicted by a calamity that impairs some of his perceptions or movements.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage makes two points. First, it addresses the principle of desire (mabda' al-shawq). The matn states that it belongs to the perceptual faculties (al-quwā al-mudrika). The commentary explains that one might mistakenly think there is another mover faculty that serves as a proximate principle for the inciting (shawqiyya) faculty, such as the power behind an animal's longing for its familiar place, a captive's desire for release, or the soul's desire for beautiful action. The author (al-Taftazani) indicates that such desires actually stem from perceptual faculties, because the principle of desire and longing (nuzū‘) is imagination (takhayyul) or intellection (ta‘aqqul). Second, the passage discusses how some of these faculties (both perceptual and mover) may be absent in certain animals or individuals, either by nature (e.g., sight in scorpions, imagination in moths, or congenital blindness in humans) or by accident (e.g., injury impairing senses or movements). This refutes the idea that all faculties are universally present.

Definition and Division of the Soul
Definition of the soul and its division into celestial, human, plant, and animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the soul as 'first perfection of a natural organic body' and its classification into four types based on functions.

Core Idea
The soul is a first perfection, classified by nutrition, sensation, reason, or circular motion.

This passage lays out the foundational definition of the soul used in Islamic philosophy. The soul is not a separate substance but the first perfection of a living body. It then explains how this definition yields four distinct types of soul corresponding to different living beings. Understanding this classification is essential for later discussions on the soul's nature and capacities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get a sense of the author's classification.
Notice the concise definition and the fourfold division.
2
Identify the general definition of the soul
Find the phrase 'كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي' (first perfection of a natural organic body). This is the core definition that applies to all types of souls.
Contrast with the idea of 'abstract' (مجرد) – only celestial and human souls are fully abstract.
3
List the four types and their functions
The passage enumerates plant (nutrition/growth), animal (sensation/motion), human (intellection of universals), and celestial (will of circular motion) souls. Note the specific function for each.
The celestial soul's function is tied to the motion of the heavens.
4
Examine the condition for terrestrial souls
The text adds that for terrestrial souls (plant, animal, human) there is an additional condition: 'possessing life in potentiality' (ذي حياة بالقوة). Consider why this is needed.
Terrestrial souls require a body with potential life, whereas celestial souls are always actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المقالة الثانية فيما يتعلق بالمجردات وفيها فصلان
الفصل الأول في النفس وفيه مباحث :
المبحث الأول : أنها تنقسم إلى فلكية وإنسانية، وقد تطلق على ما ليس بمجرد كالنفس النباتية لمبدأ آثار النبات والحيوانية لمبدأ آثار الحيوان . فمن ها هنا تفسر بأنها كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي. فمن حيث التغذي والنمو نباتية، ومن حيث الحس والحركة حيوانية، ومن حيث تعقل الكليات إنسانية، ومن حيث إرادة الحركة المستديرة فلكية، إذا جعلنا الكواكب والتداوير ونحوهما بمنزلة الآلات ويزاد لتخصيص الأرضية . قيد ذي حياة بالقوة ]
He said: The second discourse on what pertains to abstract things, and it contains two chapters. The first chapter on the soul (nafs), and it contains investigations: The first investigation: That it [the soul] is divided into celestial (falakiyya) and human (insāniyya). And it is sometimes applied to what is not abstract, like the plant soul for the principle of the effects of plants, and the animal soul for the principle of the effects of animals. So from here it is interpreted as a first perfection (kamāl awwal) for a natural organic body. So with respect to nutrition and growth it is a plant soul; with respect to sensation and movement it is an animal soul; with respect to intellection of universals it is a human soul; with respect to the will of circular motion it is a celestial soul, if we make the planets and the orbs and the like as instruments. And added for the specification of the terrestrial [soul] is the condition of possessing life in potentiality (dhī ḥayāt bil-quwwa).
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. The author begins by stating that the soul is divided primarily into celestial and human, but the term is also applied to plant and animal souls, which are not abstract. He then defines the soul in general as 'the first perfection of a natural organic body.' This definition is then used to classify four types: plant soul (concerned with nutrition and growth), animal soul (sensation and voluntary movement), human soul (intellection of universals), and celestial soul (will of circular motion). For the terrestrial soul (plant, animal, human), an additional condition is added: 'possessing life in potentiality.' The passage thus provides a comprehensive definition and classification of the soul based on its functions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of the soul as first perfection applies only to the human soul.
Correction: The definition applies to all types: plant, animal, human, and celestial souls, with the condition of 'possessing life in potentiality' for terrestrial ones.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What specific step will you take to reorient that relationship starting today?

The Soul as First Perfection of a Natural Organic Body
Detailed explanation of the soul's definition and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the soul in Islamic philosophy, including the meaning of 'first perfection', 'natural organic body', and 'possessing life in potentiality', as well as how this definition excludes other entities.

Core Idea
The soul is not a substance but a relational perfection that completes a body capable of life.

In this lesson, we dissect the classical definition of the soul given by philosophers like Aristotle and Avicenna. The passage distinguishes between soul and intellect, refines the notion of 'perfection', and clarifies why the body is included in the definition. Special attention is given to the condition 'possessing life in potentiality' which ensures the definition covers plant and animal souls while excluding celestial souls.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish soul and intellect
Read how an abstract substance attached to the body as manager is a soul; otherwise it is an intellect. Note that 'soul' is also used for material principles.
The soul is defined by its relation to the body.
2
Identify the definition and its components
Find the phrase 'first perfection for a natural organic body possessing life in potentiality'. Break down each term.
Perfection (kamal) means what completes the species in essence or attributes.
3
Understand the objection about movement
The author anticipates an objection: movement was called a first perfection. The answer explains the two perspectives: relative to potentiality vs. essence.
Movement is a first perfection for the potential, second for the body.
4
Analyze the meaning of 'body' and 'organic'
The body in the definition is taken as genus, not as matter. 'Organic' means having instruments or powers. These conditions exclude simple bodies and minerals.
The soul is form, not a perfection that makes the body a species.
5
Examine the condition 'possessing life in potentiality'
This condition does not require the body to be actually alive. It means potential for life actions, which includes plant and animal souls and excludes the celestial soul.
The celestial soul is perpetually actual, so it is excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولهما في النفس، والثاني في العقل، لما عرفت من أن الجوهر المجرد إن تعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف فنفس وإلا فعقل وقد يطلق لفظ النفس على ما ليس بمجرد ، بل مادي كالنفس النباتية التي هي مبدأ أفاعيله من التغذية والتنمية ، والتوليد . والنفس الحيوانية التي هي مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية، وتجعل النفس الأرضية اسماً لهما، أو للنفس الناطقة الإنسانية، فتفسر بأنها كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة، والمراد بالكمال ما يكمل به النوع في ذاته، ويسمى كمالاً أولاً كهيئة السيف للحديد، أو في صفاته ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً، كسائر ما يتبع النوع من العوارض مثل القطع للسيف والحركة للجسم، والعلم للإنسان فإن قيل : قد سبق أن الحركة كمال أول . قلنا : نعم بالنظر إلى ما هو بالقوة من حيث هو بالقوة، فإنه أول ما يحصل له الفرق بين أن بعد ما لم يكن وإما بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم فكمال ثان والمراد بالجسم ههنا الجنس. أعني. المأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، بل مع تجويز يقارنه غيره، أو لا يقارنه لأنها الطبيعة الجنسية الناقصة، التي إنما تتم وتكمل نوعاً بانضمام الفصل إليه لا المأخوذ ، بشرط أن يكون وحده ، لأنها مادة متقدمة بالوجود على النوع غير محمولة عليه، والنفس بالنسبة إليه صورة لا كمال يجعله نوعاً بالفعل، وقد سبق تحقيقها ذلك في بحث الماهية، وإنما أخذ الجسم في تعريف النفس لأنه اسم لمفهوم إضافي هو مبدأ صدور أفاعيل الحياة عن الجسم من غير نظر إلى كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً مجرداً أو مادياً، فلا بد من أخذه في تعريف النفس، لا من حيث ذاتها، بل من حيث تلك العلاقة لها كالبناء في تعريف الباني، والمراد بالطبيعي، ما يقابل الصناعي، وبالآلي ما يكون له قوى، وآلات مثل الغاذية والنامية ونحو ذلك ، فخرج بالقيود السابقة الكمالات الثانية، وكمالات المجردات والأعراض وهيئات المركبات الصناعية، وبالآلي صور البسائط والمعدنيات. إذ ليس فعلها بالآلات لا يقال قيد ذي حياة بالقوة مغن عن ذلك. لأنا نقول . ليس معناه أن يكون ذلك الجسم حياً، ولا أن يصدر عنه أفعال الحياة، وإلا لم يصدق التعريف إلا على النفس الإنسانية، دون النباتية والحيوانية، بل أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يصدر عنه بعض أفعال الأحياء، وإن لم يتوقف على الحياة، ولا خفاء في أن البسائط والمعدنيات كذلك. جميع وفائدة هذا القيد الاحتراز عن النفس السماوية عند من يرى أن النفس إنما هي للفلك الكلي، وإن ما فيه من الكواكب والأفلاك الجزئية بمنزلة آلات له، فتكون جسماً آلياً، إلا أن ما يصدر عنه من التعقلات والحركات الإرادية، التي هي من أفاعيل الحياة تكون دائماً، وبالفعل لا كأفاعيل النبات والحيوان من التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل، والإدراك، والحركة الإرادية والنطق .
The first of them [the two chapters] is on the soul, and the second on the intellect, because you have known that the abstract substance (jawhar mujarrad), if it is attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition, then it is a soul; otherwise it is an intellect. And the word 'soul' is sometimes applied to what is not abstract, but rather material, like the plant soul which is the principle of its actions—nutrition, growth, and reproduction—and the animal soul which is the principle of sensation and voluntary movement. And the terrestrial soul is made a name for both, or for the speaking human soul. So it is interpreted as a first perfection for a natural organic body possessing life in potentiality. And what is meant by perfection (kamāl) is that by which the species is completed in its essence, and it is called a first perfection, like the shape of the sword for iron; or in its attributes, and it is called a second perfection, like the rest of the accidents that follow the species, such as cutting for the sword, movement for the body, and knowledge for the human. For if it is said: 'Movement has already preceded as a first perfection.' We say: Yes, with respect to what is in potentiality in as much as it is in potentiality, for it is the first thing that occurs to it; the difference is that after not being, it is a first perfection, but with respect to the essence of the body, it is a second perfection. And what is meant by body here is the genus—that is, taken not with the condition that it is alone or not alone, but with allowance that something else accompanies it or does not accompany it, because it is the deficient generic nature which is only completed as a species by the addition of the differentia; not taken with the condition that it is alone, because it is matter prior in existence to the species, not predicated of it. And the soul with respect to it is a form, not a perfection that makes it a species in act. This has already been verified in the discussion of quiddity. And the body is taken in the definition of the soul only because it is a name for a relational concept: the principle of the emergence of life's actions from the body, without consideration of whether it is a substance or an accident, abstract or material. So it must be taken in the definition of the soul, not in terms of its essence, but in terms of that relation, like 'building' in the definition of 'builder'. And what is meant by 'natural' is what is opposed to artificial; and by 'organic' is that which has powers and instruments, such as the nutritive and the growth [powers] and the like. So by the previous restrictions, the second perfections, the perfections of abstract things, accidents, and forms of artificial composites are excluded; and by 'organic', the forms of simple bodies and minerals are excluded, because they do not act by instruments. It cannot be said that the condition 'possessing life in potentiality' suffices for that. For we say: Its meaning is not that the body is alive, nor that the actions of life actually issue from it; otherwise the definition would apply only to the human soul, not to the plant and animal souls. Rather, it is that it is such that some of the actions of living beings can possibly issue from it, even if it does not depend on life. And it is no secret that simple bodies and minerals are also like that. All [this], and the benefit of this condition is to guard against the celestial soul according to those who hold that the soul belongs only to the universal sphere, and that the planets and partial spheres in it are like instruments for it, so it is an organic body, except that what issues from it—intellections and voluntary movements, which are among the actions of life—are perpetual and in act, unlike the actions of plants and animals: nutrition, growth, reproduction of like, perception, voluntary movement, and speech.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the definition of the soul. It begins by distinguishing between soul and intellect: an abstract substance attached to the body with management is a soul; otherwise it is an intellect. The term 'soul' is also used for material principles like plant and animal souls. The full definition is 'first perfection of a natural organic body possessing life in potentiality'. The author explains 'perfection' as that which completes the species in its essence (first perfection, like shape of sword) or in its attributes (second perfection, like cutting). An objection is raised: movement was previously said to be a first perfection. The answer clarifies that movement is a first perfection relative to potentiality, but relative to the body's essence it is a second perfection. Next, the meaning of 'body' in the definition is discussed: it is taken as genus, not as matter. The soul is a form that makes the body a species in act, but the body in the definition is the generic nature that acquires completion through the soul. The body is included because it is a relational concept: the soul is defined by its relation to the body as the principle of life's actions. The terms 'natural' (opposed to artificial) and 'organic' (having instruments) are explained. These conditions exclude second perfections, abstract perfections, accidents, artificial forms, simple bodies, and minerals. The condition 'possessing life in potentiality' is then defended: it does not mean the body is actually alive, but that it is potentially capable of producing vital actions. This allows the definition to include plant and animal souls. The benefit of this condition is to exclude the celestial soul, which is perpetually actual in its vital actions, unlike terrestrial souls.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition 'possessing life in potentiality' means the body must be actually alive.
Correction: The text explains it means the body is capable of producing life actions in potentiality, not that it is alive. This allows the definition to cover plant and animal souls.

Checkpoint
Why is the condition 'possessing life in potentiality' added to the definition of the soul?
A) To exclude the celestial soul
B) To ensure the body is actually alive
C) To include only the human soul
D) To exclude plant and animal souls

Key Takeaway
The soul is defined as the first perfection of a natural organic body capable of life in potentiality, a definition that includes plant and animal souls while excluding the always-actual celestial soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage defines the soul as the first perfection that makes you human, while your actions (like knowledge) are second perfections. Look at your daily life: are you investing more in refining your second perfections (skills, achievements) or in nurturing that first perfection—your very essence as a soul? What would change if you prioritized the latter starting today?
?
The condition 'possessing life in potentiality' means your soul is not yet fully actualized. In what specific areas of your spiritual and moral life are you still in a state of potentiality? What is one concrete step you can take this week to actualize that potential, rather than remaining passive?
?
The soul is defined as the principle of management and disposition of the body. Are you truly managing your body—its desires, habits, and actions—as a trust from God? Where have you surrendered that management to fleeting impulses? What would it mean for you to reclaim that governance today?

The Definition of the Soul: First Perfection of a Natural Organic Body
The author defines the soul and explains each qualifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the soul as 'first perfection of a natural organic body' and the purpose of each qualifier.

Core Idea
The soul is a first perfection that gives life to a natural body with organs.

The author begins by clarifying the definition of the soul. He explains that 'body' here refers to the genus, not the fully constituted species, and that each qualifier serves to exclude certain types of beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the definition: 'first perfection of a natural organic body'.
Notice how the author breaks down each part.
2
Understand the qualifiers
Identify the three qualifiers: 'natural', 'organic', and 'possessing life potentially' (the latter is covered in next lesson). Focus on 'natural' and 'organic' for now.
'Natural' excludes artificial; 'organic' excludes simple bodies and minerals.
3
See what is excluded
List what is excluded by each qualifier: second perfections, abstract entities, accidents, artificial compounds (by 'natural'); forms of simple bodies and minerals (by 'organic').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن بعد ما لم يكن وإما بالنظر إلى ذات الجسم فكمال ثان والمراد بالجسم ههنا الجنس. أعني. المأخوذ لا بشرط أن يكون وحده أو لا وحده، بل مع تجويز يقارنه غيره، أو لا يقارنه لأنها الطبيعة الجنسية الناقصة، التي إنما تتم وتكمل نوعاً بانضمام الفصل إليه لا المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده، لأنها مادة متقدمة بالوجود على النوع غير محمولة عليه، والنفس بالنسبة إليه صورة لا كمال يجعله نوعاً بالفعل، وقد سبق تحقيقها ذلك في بحث الماهية، وإنما أخذ الجسم في تعريف النفس لأنه اسم لمفهوم إضافي هو مبدأ صدور أفاعيل الحياة عن الجسم من غير نظر إلى كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً مجرداً أو مادياً، فلا بد من أخذه في تعريف النفس، لا من حيث ذاتها، بل من حيث تلك العلاقة لها كالبناء في تعريف الباني، والمراد بالطبيعي، ما يقابل الصناعي، وبالآلي ما يكون له قوى، وآلات مثل الغاذية والنامية ونحو ذلك، فخرج بالقيود السابقة الكمالات الثانية، وكمالات المجردات والأعراض وهيئات المركبات الصناعية، وبالآلي صور البسائط والمعدنيات. إذ ليس فعلها بالآلات لا يقال قيد ذي حياة بالقوة مغن عن ذلك. لأنا نقول. ليس معناه أن يكون ذلك الجسم حياً، ولا أن يصدر عنه أفعال الحياة، وإلا لم يصدق التعريف إلا على النفس الإنسانية، دون النباتية والحيوانية، بل أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يصدر عنه بعض أفعال الأحياء، وإن لم يتوقف على الحياة، ولا خفاء في أن البسائط والمعدنيات كذلك. وفائدة هذا القيد الاحتراز عن النفس السماوية عند من يرى أن النفس إنما هي للفلك الكلي، وإن ما فيه من الكواكب والأفلاك الجزئية بمنزلة آلات له، فتكون جسماً آلياً، إلا أن ما يصدر عنه من التعقلات والحركات الإرادية، التي هي من أفاعيل الحياة تكون دائماً، وبالفعل لا كأفاعيل النبات والحيوان من التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل، والإدراك، والحركة الإرادية والنطق. أعني تعقل الكليات، فإنها ليست دائمة، بل قد تكون بالقوة، وأما عند من يرى أن لكل كرة نفساً، وأنها ليست من الأجسام الآلية، فلا حاجة إلى هذا القيد، ولهذا لم يذكره الأكثرون. وذهب أبو البركات إلى أنه إنما يذكر عوض قولهم: آلي فيقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم ذي حياة بالقوة وعبارة القدماء: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي، واحترز بطبيعي عن الكمالات الصناعية، كالتشكيلات الحاصلة بفعل الإنسان. ثم قال: وقد يقال كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي بتأخير طبيعي، وهو إما غلط في النقل، وإما مقصود به المعنى الذي ذكرنا. فظهر أن ما يقال من أن بعضهم رفع طبيعي صفة لكمال، ليس معناه أنه يرفع مع التأخير صفة لكمال، ويخفض بعده آلي صفة لجسم، فإنه في غاية القبح، وكذا لو رفع حينئذ آلي أيضاً، صفة مع ذكر ذي حياة صفة لجسم، بل معناه أنه يقدم فيرفع على ما قال الإمام أن بعضهم جعل الطبيعي صفة للكمال فقال: كمال أول طبيعي لجسم أول آلي.
That after it was not, or with respect to the essence of the body, then a second perfection; and what is meant by 'body' here is the genus, meaning [the body] taken not conditionally that it is by itself or not by itself, but with the possibility that something else accompanies it or does not accompany it, because it is the deficient generic nature, which is only completed and perfected as a species by the attachment of the differentia to it, not [the body] taken conditionally that it is by itself, because it is matter prior in existence to the species, not predicated upon it; and the soul in relation to it is a form, not a perfection that makes it an actual species; and this has already been investigated in the discussion of quiddity. And the body is only taken in the definition of the soul because it is a name for an additional concept, which is the principle of the issuance of vital actions from the body without consideration of whether it is a substance or an accident, abstract or material; so it must be taken in the definition of the soul, not with respect to its essence but with respect to that relation, like 'builder' in the definition of 'builder'. And what is meant by 'natural' is what is opposed to artificial; and by 'organic' is what has powers and instruments, such as the nutritive, the growth, and so on. So by the previous restrictions are excluded the second perfections, the perfections of abstract entities and accidents and the dispositions of artificial compounds; and by 'organic' are excluded the forms of simple bodies and minerals, since their action is not by instruments. It may not be said: the qualification 'possessing life potentially' is sufficient instead of that. For we say: it does not mean that the body is living, nor that it issues vital actions; otherwise the definition would only apply to the human soul, not to the vegetative and animal; rather it means that it is such that some vital actions can possibly issue from it, even if not dependent on life; and it is not hidden that simple bodies and minerals are not like that. And the benefit of this qualification is to guard against the celestial soul, according to the one who holds that the soul is only for the universal sphere, and that the planets and partial spheres are like instruments for it, so it is an organic body; except that what issues from it of intellections and voluntary movements, which are among vital actions, are perpetual and actual, not like the actions of plants and animals of nutrition, growth, reproduction, perception, voluntary movement, and speech. I mean intellection of universals, for they are not perpetual, but may be potential. But according to the one who holds that each sphere has a soul, and that they are not among organic bodies, then there is no need for this qualification; and for this reason most do not mention it. And Abu al-Barakat held that it is only mentioned instead of their saying 'organic', so it is said: first natural perfection for a body possessing life potentially; and the expression of the ancients: first natural perfection for an organic body. And he guarded by 'natural' against artificial perfections, like the formations resulting from human action. Then he said: It may be said 'first perfection for an organic natural body' with a delay of 'natural'; and this is either a mistake in transmission, or intended to mean that which we have mentioned. So it appears that what is said, that some made 'natural' an adjective of 'perfection', does not mean that he raises it with delay as an adjective of 'perfection', and lowers 'organic' afterwards as an adjective of 'body', for that is extremely ugly; likewise if he then also raises 'organic' as an adjective, with the mention of 'possessing life' as an adjective of 'body'; rather it means that he advances and raises it, according to what the Imam said: that some made 'natural' an adjective of 'perfection' and said: first natural perfection for a first organic body.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage defines the soul (nafs) with the definition 'first perfection of a natural organic body' (kamal awwal tabi'i li jism ali). The author first clarifies 'body' (jism): it means the genus, taken without condition (la bi shart), i.e., the generic nature that is completed by the differentia. The soul is related to it as form, not as a perfection that actualizes the species. The body is included because the soul is the principle of vital actions from a body, so the definition must refer to this relation, just as 'builder' is used in defining a builder. Then 'natural' (tabi'i) is defined as opposed to artificial, and 'organic' (ali) as having instruments like nutritive and growth powers. These qualifiers exclude second perfections, perfections of abstract substances, accidents, artificial forms (via 'natural'), and forms of simple bodies and minerals (via 'organic'). The author then explains the qualifier 'possessing life potentially' (dhi hayatin bi-l-quwwa): it does not mean the body is actually alive, but that it can issue vital actions; otherwise the definition would only fit humans. Its benefit is to exclude the celestial soul according to the view that the soul is only for the universal sphere and that partial spheres are its instruments. However, those who hold each sphere has its own soul do not need this qualifier. The author then discusses Abu al-Barakat's alternative phrasing and a grammatical point about word order, concluding that the intended meaning is clear.

كمال أول

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the soul is a second perfection or an accident.
Correction: The passage shows it is a first perfection of a natural organic body, not a second perfection or an accident.

What is excluded by the qualifier 'organic' (āli) in the definition of the soul?

Key Takeaway
The soul's definition precisely specifies its role as the principle of life in a body with organs, excluding all non-living and artificial forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your soul is the first perfection of your natural organic body. In your daily life, do you more often act as if your body is a mere machine or as if it is the organic instrument of your soul? What is one specific way you can honor your body today as the expression of your soul?
?
If your soul is what makes your body alive and capable of vital actions, then every action you do—eating, moving, thinking—is an expression of your soul. Have you considered that negligence in caring for your body is negligence toward your soul? Will you commit to one act of physical care this week as a spiritual act?
?
The passage distinguishes between first perfection (soul) and second perfections like knowledge. Are you living as if acquiring knowledge is more important than actualizing your soul? What would change if you prioritized the soul's first perfection in your daily choices?
